PLACE GF ANTALYA PROJECT IN THE DEVELOPMENT
OF TURKEY 'S FOREST
Background
Although 'Turkey's forests are amoong the largest in Europe - with
an estimated 19.2 million ha, they are in fourth place after Spain (26.7 million
ha), Sweden (23.4 million ha) and Finland (21.7 million ha) - they continue
to be managed, as they 'have been for the past century, for conservation
rather than production goals. The annual cut of commercial wood from the
Turkish forests in 1970 amounted to 0.22 m3/ha, whereas in Spain, Sweden
ar,d Finland it was 0.3 m3/ha, 2,4 m3/ha and 1.7 m3/ha respectively, even
though growth rates in the Scandinavian countries tend to be lower. 1/
Turkish forestry officials reinforce the conservationist tradition
by administering both the economic and social life of persons living in
forest areas. Reasons why the forest system is reluctant to change its
administration and management are:
- Forest dweller's rights to grazing
- Forest products and material assistance
are guaranteed by law - even, in part,
by the constitution
- Forest dwellers make up over 35% of the
population (some 13 million).
Turkey has 15,000 small, weak and scattered separate forest indus-
try establishments. A few new plants including three pulp and paper plants
use up-to-date technology and produce quality products which meet world
standards. Yet, the mills are too small by world standards and will have
difficulty in meeting foreign competition. Secondly, the new mills lack
positive control over wood supplies.
In order to achieve economies in production costs it has now
become essential to buiLd plants with higher outputs. The average size of
particle board plants establ.ished in Turkey since 1970 has been 14,000 tons
of product capacity while each of the pulip and paper plawnts are of Ness than
100,000 tons of paper and paperboarc6 capac_ty per annunm. .  n. contrasts new
particle board mills ara beinz, built in Europe and iNorth America of 100,000
tons to 250,000 tons per anmim.  Similarly, most new pulp and paper mills
are in the range of 200,000 to 350,000 tons per year.
1 /     m3 Gt wood refers to solid measure uncer bark throughout the report
unless otherzwise noted.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
The small size of forest-based industrial plants is due mainly to:
- A lack of guaranteed raw material supply
- A lack of capital
- A lack of foreign competition
Previously, the high prices of wood in Turkey as compared with world
prices of wood were a major concern. Wood prices were loaded with "'social
c:, )ss" because the Ministry of Forestry had to install infrastructure (roads,
co r,uancations) in non-productive forest areas. This situation is not so
serious today, and wood prices are not burdened with high costs, but prices
rema,in high in view of the strong demand and limited supply (see also
Chapter 8).
The need for change is importantly emphasized in looking at the
mirzor contribution of forestry to the economy. In 1972, the forestty sector
con$ributed about TL 5,700 million or 3.7% to GN'P._J About 20% of the net
value of production was unprocessed, a further 54% was processed and the
rema.nder was converted into paper products. The net values of production
are estimated as follows:

e Antalya forest region and other forestry sectOrs, Jn order to encoar-
age development.  These were substantiated and furthered by UND?/FAO projects

From 1961 to 1965, the UNDP/FAO "Pre-Investment Survey of the Antalya
Region" included a survey and inventory of the Antalya/Isparta forests. it
identified a forest industrial project capable of producing 50,000 m3 of
sawnwood and 100,000 tons of kraft pulp annually, and recommended a more
rational approach to forest management, clear felling and reforestation.
In 1969, a Finnish firm of forest industrial consuItants .
(Jaako Poyry and Co.) did a pre-Investment study on f"orestry and forest
industries development in the Antalya area.  Based on a given wood susply of
one amiLlion m3 year, the consultants concluded that a feasibility study was
needed on a complex producing 100,000 tons/a of kraft liner, 100,000 tons/'a
of kraft sack and wrapping paper and 150,000 m3/a of sawnwood situated near
Manavgat on the Antalya coast.
Bank Role
The Bank became involved in the Antalya Project first by preparing
detailed worklists for forestry activities whnen the FAO/I3RD cooperative pro-
gram began project preparation work in 1969. This was after completion of
the Poyry feasibility report which was later termed invalid because no de-
tailed worklists were contained in the terms of reference,
Despite follow-up work, several factors preceding 1969, a main
one being major organizational changes going on in SEKA and the Ministry of
Forestry, were detrimental to any progress in forestry work.
- The Ministry of Forestry saw no need for the
work (but assurances for only 600,000 m3/yr
were given)
-  SEKA felt Poyry study was sufficient
- Lack of coordination as observed by two
Bank missions in 1971, who came in response
to Government requests for Bank financing.
The second Bank mission revealed past errors and was the pivot
upon which a fresh, realistic approach was made. To help solve one of the
largest problems, lack of coordination, a Turkish Project Preparation Team
comprised of staff seconded from both SEKA and the Ministry of Forestry
was set up to rework the feasibility study, with the widest terms of refer-
ence. The insistence was that the 'Turkish Team be solidly backed up by a
team of foreign experts from a company well reputed for its work in inte-
grated forest utilization projects. An initial resistance was turned into
enthusiasm when the Turkish Team went to W.iqshington to discuss its terms of
reference and then visited modern forestry operations in British Columbia,
It was made clear how obsolete Turkey's systems were.

Page 4
The Teamn persuaded the Government to apply for UNDP financing for
the consultants and insisted that the Bank be the executing agency.  By
ciexplanation of the role of the new study, especially by showing the
-.ee of- ±e:al of its terms of reference, tTNDP and FAO changed from re-
faSe to enthusiastic support.  As a result, high level technical staff
L<r5rz Don a8ncies have beer. closely inivolved, and givern valuable advice,
as a." stages of the study.
Lnvitaziona to suin:lt proposals were sent to four major cons-alting
c<vpan'es ~n YMovember 1971  and by April 1972, Sandwell and Company of Van-
CoUVe-r  Canada had been appointed and its field team was in operation.
rLn the ter-ms of reference, careful attention was paid to the
administration of the study as well as ensuring that all technical and economic
areas were covered.  Three review meet4.ngs during the work. assured agreement of
the Government and the Bank at each major stage at which techno-economie para-
ieters would be identified and fixed.
The procedure worked very well, the result being that the project
is now thoroughly prepared and underway. At the same time, a major change
in institutional framework is being introduced - gently but firmly - to
ensure that SEKA and the Ministry of Forestry are jointly cooperating in
necessary activities at least at the working level.
Definition of Project Parameters
The investigations of the technical and economical. parameters of
the project proceeded on three parallel paths.
1.   The forest resource and inventory was thoroughly
researched to find what size and type of industry
it could support.
2.   The market needs were studied to find the national
needs and the place for the products that could be
made from the forest.
3.   The existing infrastructure and cost levels were
found to get a feel for the type, size and loca-
tion of industrial plants.
Durirg the first review meeting, the bas_c alterratives of wood
supply for new industrZai use were agreed upon and defined as follows:

Page 5
Case I
The wood supply would consist of the Annual Allowable Cut 1/ (AAC) from
Antalya plus additional wood from Isparta, with all the present sales of
wood both within Antalya and out of Antalya to be continued at the present
level.
Case II
The Antalya region wood supply would be the same as Case T but statutory
sales of wood would be allowed.
Case III
The wood supply, sawdust plus supplemental wood chips would be the same as
Case II and could come from new sawmills planned for the Adana/Mersin area.
Some 200,000 m3/yr of pVlpable fibre was estimated to be available from this
source.
Case IV
The wood supply would be the same as Case III reduced by allowing present
sales of wood within the Antalya region to continue. Export of wood from
the region would be discontinued.
In all the above mentioned cases, the statutory suppl y of wood to
forest villagers would be provided. For each case the log production was
based on an AAC for-Antalya of 710,000 m3/yr. The volume of logs produced
in each case was as follows:

In the meantime, products to De produced from Antalya were quickly
narrowed to unbleached kraft paper products and sawn lumber.  Bleached paper
and newsprint were well planned for in existing or plarnned SEKA plants,
wnereas a shortfall for kraft papers like linerboard and sackkraft would
eventuate if capacity was not introduced soon. Plywood was not considered
sinrce there are practically no peeler logs in the Antalya inventory.  Particle
board wa:s omitted because there was already sufficient capacity instalied
or planne& and because there are technical difficulties producing it fromn
Pinus Brutia.

(a) Different plant locations, including the possibility

Costs and benefits would be developed and financial and economic
rates of return for eachn case would be calculated with comparative accuracy.
On this basis, the next review meeting hoped to decide on the one case out
of Sandwell's 29 meriting detailed studies as THE Project.
The two main choices for plant site narrowed to Manavgat and Antalya.
There were considerable problems with water suppliy at the latter but it was
considered for comparative purposes if nothing else. The possibility of one
or more smaller sawmills remote from the central mill was also considered.
Comparative estimates for production, costs, income and rates o&
return are shown in Table 2 on rough basis> but are accurate enough for inter-
case comparison. The results were differentiated enough to guide the final
choice of the "best case". They showed that:
Integrated sawmill/pulp and paper mills were
financially and economically viable.
= Sawmills with chip production were not financially
viable although by a Quirk of the present system
of price levels in Turkey showed extraordinary high
economic returns. 1/
= There was not much economic or financial difference
between cases where all activities were centralized
and those with remote sawmills, nor between plants
in Antalya or Manavgat. On the other hand, there
were clear technical and administrative reasons for
locating at Manavgar and for centralizing all
activities.
-  The grea_er the volume of wood processed the better
the return.
} X   Th.e anomaly is due to the fact that sawn lumber is priced generally at
about world muarket prices, Wnile paper has about twice the world market
price.  A saw miliL would only have a financial income equal to its eco-
naom-c income, but mts fica cial costs aere much higher than its economic
sot because of high tax levels in cost inputs.

The meeting was therefore able to outline the final case as follows:
- The Case IV wood supply would be used to maximize
production level but not to eliminate present wood
sales in the Antalya region.
-  The Manavgat site, with centralized facilities, was
chosen but a comparison of equal accuracy would be
made in moving to an inland site at Manavgat.
Two products situations would be studied - the first
with linerboard the only paper product and the second
with 1/3 of the paper production as sackkraft. In both
cases lumber would also be produced.
As work on the final case progressed, Sandwell and SEKA dug into the
finer details of market demand and wood availability and soon uncovered that
undue emphasis was placed on linerboard and lumber production. Sackkraft, they
found, would be in heavy demand in the near future, a-nd the expansion of Cay-
cuma would not be started for sometime. Moreover, the sawmill capacity was
enormous - nearly eight times bigger than the then biggest sawmill in Turkey.
SEKA felt the mill's paper capacity should therefore be increased, its lumber
capacity decreased, and adequate supplies of sackkraft should be made available.
At this time, one further complication crept in as doubts began
to mount over the viability of SEKA's bleached pulp project at Balikesir.
Severe criticism by the UNDP/FAO team forced a re-examination of thrie project
and showed that - besides a number of other detrimental factors - there would
be almost unsurmountable difficulties in effluent disposal to modern standards.
If the Balikesir mill could produce unbleached products the problem would not
be so severe. In this case, the Antalya mill should be switched to bleached
grades. They decided to examine this possibility. SEKA commissioned Sandwell
independently of the UNDP/IBRD project to produce comparative profitability
figures for bleached versus unbleached production at Antalya.
Sandwell undertook the assignment but pointed out that they could
not vouch for the production levels set by SEKA for the bleached case (which
would produce pulp as well as paper). The key result of the comparison is
given in Table 3.

The results show there is little to choose between the bleached
and unbleached cases, on the basis of profitability. There are, however,
overwhelming arguments that disfavor the bleached case:
-  The Antalya project would be delayed by up to one
year while thorough market studies on bleached
pulp and paper were made.
- The source of short fibre furnish was also
assessed (SEKA had said the reed and straw mill
proposed for Afyon would be used) and the source
of new unbleached capacity was defined.
- It was by no means sure these two latter points
would be satisfactorily settled.
- A bleached mill in Antalya would have a more serious
effluent disposal problem than an unbleached mill
and might not be compatible with proposed tourism
development.
For these and other reasons, the bleached case was therefore dropped
for Antalya. Bleached products would still be needed of course and their
source of supply will be studied in a major study of the Marmara region 'see
Annex 6).  In the meantime, an Antalya, attention was focused on the
production levels for a plant producing lumber, sackkraft and linerboard.
First, the sawmill capacity was set to process some 300,000 m 3/yr
of logs using only the best sawlogs (minimum 20 cm top diameter), thereby
getting a higher recovery (and higher profit) than if all sawlogs were used.
It would also reduce investment cost but not preclude the possibility of 3an
expansion later if more wood becomes available. A capacity of 182,000 m /yr
of lumber production appeared the most suitable.
Next, the linerboard and sackkraft capacities were set to satisfy
the following criteria:
- The wood available to the project should
be fully utilized.
-  The paper would be produced by alternate
runs on a single paper machine.
The two criteria showed that running on linerboard only, the mill
could produce 155,000 TPA, and on sackkraft only 90,000 TPA. Production
schedules were then established, aimed rirst at satisfying the more urgent
demand for sackkraft. However, once linerboard was produced, in no subse-
quent year would the production be less than in a former year (i.e., con-
vertors who set themselves up would be assured of supplies).

The production plan shows that additional sackkraft capacity would
be needed in the nation by 1986. It should be realized, however, that pla7ns
of this nature can only be indicative and that new facts are always arising.
For instance, the nation is not forced to install new sackkraft capacity in
1986 - then it might find it more convenient and economical to invest in
facilities fcr bulk handling of cement thus actually leveling or even de-
creasing sackkraft demand (it is indeed recommended that this alternative
be seriously studied).
The key point is that with a multipurpose paper machine "With back-
up pulp-making facilities), the Antalya mill will be able to handle most
situations that develop.  It will certa-ialy give enough capacity for the next
8 years so that proper planning o$f  uture capacity for unbleached kraZt papers
can be done.
On this basis, the Sandwel proposals for plant capacity, product
type, production schedule and general plant type were accepted and approved
at the final major policy review meeting, which included Government repre-
sentatives at the end of the study in January 1973.  It is on these proposals
too - with minor revisions - that this appraisal report is based.

INSTITUTIONAL BACKGROUND
The two operating institutions in the forestry sector are the
Turkish State Pulp and Paper Enterprise (Seluloz ve Ka-it - SEKA) and the
Ministry of Forestry. In this Chapter the two institutions are described
in detail and conclusions are drawn on how they will be affected by the
Antalya Project. A change in their usual method of coordination is also
described.
A.   Ministry of Forestry
History and Organization
Forestry is an old, established sector of the Turkish economy. In
1868, a decree was promulgated to ensure conservation of the forests. Villagers
had, for generations, cut wood in the communal forests to cover their needs,
but toward the end of the Ottoman Empire the control of the State over its
forestry waned. This led to excessive cutting and grazing and a general
degeneration of the country's forest resources. In 1937, the Forestry Depart-
ment was charged with control of the felling and marketing of logs. In 1944,
all privately owned forest land in excess of 5 ha was nationalized. In
1956, the law was modified to include all forested land in excess of 3 ha.
To compensate the forest villagers for this loss of income, the law obliged
the state to provide them with their needs in fuelwood and industrial wood
at 1/10 of the auction "reserve prices".
The Government established a General Directorate of Forestry in
1957 under the Ministry of Agriculture which was charged with: (a) the
preservation of the national forests; (b) increasing yields through manage-
ment and development; (c) planting trees; and (d) educating personnel.
In 1969, forest administration was transferred to a new Ministry
of Forests with four general Directorates:
(a) Forestry;
(b) Erosion Control and Reforestation;
(c) Forest Villages; and
(d) Forest Products and Industries.
The General Directorate of Forestry (GDF) with over 27,000 permanent
employees is one of the largest Government agencies and is responsible for all
forestry operations and for financing. Its headquarters consist of 26 depart-



ANNEX 3
Page 2
men-s grouped into seven divisions. A majority of the Ministry's staff is
,ioyed in the field.  Administration, however, is highly centralized, and
p- rcticaily all decisions are made in Ankara.
In mountainous, remote areas, the district forester is often the
goverm=ent officer in touch with the people.  Sections, groups or
eSvresantatives of other general directorates may also be located in the
reonl.  ^,The senior representative maintains close liaison with regional
Oczf:icials 'ut he reports to his respective office in Ankara.
The MF employs over 1,500 forest officers.  Over 700 of these are
C raduates of the Forestry Faculty of Istanbul University. Nearly 100 have
post-graduate degrees from overseas universities. The junior professional
officers are graduates of the Turkish Forest Training Schools. A large
proportion of staff have considerable professional competence, but training
is often outdated. The service is well provisioned with officers and office
staff as well as with staff housing. Lack of vehicles is a constraint to the
operational efficiency of field staff.
For administrative purposes, Turkey is divided into 24 forest
Regions (basmudurluk). Each of these is divided into Divisions (isletme)
and Ranger Districts (bolge). There are 183 of the former and 1,178 of
the latter. Each "bolge" is subdivided into several "Iseries`, each with
an area between 3,000 and 6,000 ha. These are the basic units of manage-
ment of the Turkish Forests.
There is also a Forest Research Institute in Ankara and a Poplar
Research Institute in lzmit, as well as field stations.
Region
The headquarters of a typical region like Antalya comprises the
following sections:
Accounting                          Roads
Personnel                           Legal
Production Coordination             Forest Production
Maintenance Workshops               Research and Pathology
Training                            Forest Management
In. addition to a research station, representatives of the GD of
Forest VilLage Relations and a group of the GD of Afforestation and Erosion
Control are located in Antalya. A MF state box plant is also located in
zha region.
The region -Is subdivided into 14 divisions. The divisions are
further divzced into 75 districts.  Two of the divisions are small in area,
-Itensively managed and intended to act as pilot areas.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
Division Organization
The Division is the responsible administrative unit for all routine
forest operations. All direct accounting, production, silviculture, road
construction and maintenance, forest protection, reforestation and other
work is directly controlled by division staff.  The division staff includes
accounting, forestry, log depot and supervisory groups.  1gletme offices
are located in subprovince towns which usually have reasonable facilities.
Planning, equipment repair, insect and pathology protectioa and road en-
gineering are not normally included in the isletme activities.
District Organization
Each isletme has several bolge or ranger districts supervised by
resident forest rangers who are responsible for all routine silviculture,
protection and production activities within their area. Forest guards
and a bolge clerk comprise the bolge staff. Bolge offices are located
in forest villages, where often community facilities are minimal.
Planning
The GDF's Forest Management branch's basic planning of all forest
activities at 10 year intervals is done in Ankara. Other similar plans
are developed by relevant GDF divisions.
After annual targets are provided by the GDF, the basmudurluk and
isletme staff prepare budgets for the work required to implement the plans
and achieve production objectives.  These are subjected for approval to
the GDF in Ankara. Activities requiring subsidization must be consolidated
on a national level. Each isletme is then responsible for achieving budget
objectives.
In essence, the two plans which now control all regional and isletme
activities are the 10-year Forest Management Plans and the Annual Budgets.
Supplementary road development and equipment maintenance planning are done
by the GDF headquarters. Annual detailed plans are done Dy the regional
staff according to directions from the GDF.
Forest Management
Until 1963, forests wAere arnge  or a district basis.  Selection
felling was applied to all species, and yiJ,~ds were based on rotations
varying between 100 and 200 years.  In 1963, a new management planning phase
was initiated. The new system is based on sustaining production on saall
un4ts (or series) of 3,Ou  - 5,000 haa.  1ong rotations (8C - 150 years) are
still used for yield calculations although 70% of the forest is already



ANNEX 3
Page 4
m'atu-ei or overmature, and the annual increment is negligible. Fifty percent
.1'U tI'se yield still derives from thinnings and selection felling. For the
Z.1 n.al cut a strip-shelter wood system is used. The system is expensive and
w.  neer harvest the overmature stock.  Natural regeneration is rarely
-ueeesfaul and eventually replanting has to be done after expensive removal
o-'~ iaGligL> £eacies.
Consu7zants aud international agencies have, for some t±ne, re-
com;iaen.zde  -naz -mature and overmature growing stock should be liquidated
ove- a period of say 50 years; that clear felling where appropriate be
pr-acticed; that management be reorganized on a isletme basis; and that cut
over areas be replanted iumediately.  The MF favor this approach in the
A.ntalya basmutdurluk (see aLso Annex 4) and has indicated that these measures,
if- shon to be appropriate, will be adopted in other basmudurluks.
Forest Industries Controlled by MF
Of t1he 5,200 sawmills in Turkey, 14 owned by the State and operated
by the MF, are larger than nonmal units.
The 14 State operated sawmills, mostly around the Black Sea area,
accounted for about 7% of the total output of sawnwood in 1968 and employed
about 3,000 workers. All but one of the sawmills operate only one shift and
consequently have a low lumber yield. Most are medium sized (log input
capacities of between 10,000 and 40,000 m3per year). One has a log capacity
of 80,000 m3.  The oldest of these mills was erected in 1940 and the most
modern in 1957. Three have already been modernized and converted from gang
mills to band mills with modern drying kilns; another three are in the process
of being modernized. Once modernization is completed, the capacity of log
input per shift will increase from 259,000 m3 to 460,000 m3.
None of the sawmills are integrated with other forest industries
to permit rational utilization of sawmill waste. The annual productivity
per man in the State sawmills is only about 62 m3.  A normal figure for
modern banddmills should be in excess 3of 100 m3;  the corresponding figure
for modern gang mills is about 170 m .
Here are some reasons for low productivity in the State Mills.
- Poor quality of raw material (mills sometimes get
auctiron logs which cn did not fetch the reserve price)
- Excess staff and labor
- High turnover of managers and technicians



ANNEX 3
Page 5
The State sawmills' production is marketed through auctions at
which quantities that average 1,000 m3 are sold in lots of about 50 to
100 m3. In spite of a somewhat cumbersome administrative procedure, the
output Is easily sold. This is because of stricter quality control than in
the small private mills and air drying of sawnwood for longer periods.
Production
During the first and second of the Governments' five year pians,
production and sales of industrial roundwood rose from a level of Just
over 1 million m3 in 1961 to 5.6 million m3 in 1972. During the same period,
fuelwood production is astimated to have r'.se.n from 8 million to 10 million m3.
Total production increased annually by 7% between 1963 and 1967 and 5.3%
between 1968 and 1972. The Government's projections for the third five year
plan anticipate a growth in domestic demand for industrial roundwood of 6.6%
annually and a slight fall in the demand for fuelwood. The forecasted
national requirements are as follows:

The Annual Allowable sut of industrial roundwood in 1982 is expected
to be in excess of 14 million m  or over twice the present cut.  Since fuel-
wood consumption already exceeds the level permitted by the Ministry of Forests'
current management plans and since the projected requirements of industrial
roundwood are greater than the net Allowable Annual Cut of 9 million m3 by
1977, urgent consideration must be given to the adoption of more productive
forest management systems and a speeding up of the afforestation program.
Budget and Finance
The budget and financial arrangements for the activities within the
Ministry of Forests can be divided into three categories, the Ministry of
Forests budget, the "Annex Budget" and the "'Revolving Fund". Considering
all three budgets together, of which the Revolving Fund is by far the
largest, the Ministry of Forests has been contributing to the national bud-
get in the past years, particularly in the formx of corporation taxes and pay-
ments to the export fund.  The budget amournts for these contributions have
been as follows (actual amounts in 1972 and 1973 are expected to be much
higher):

These amounts represent net contributions except in 1972 and 1973
when small allocations from the national budget have taken place (1972:  TL
3 million; 1973: TL 20 million) to cover the relatively unimportant budget
of the Ministry of Forests itself, which was separated from the Ministry of
Agriculture in 1971.
In addition to the payments of these taxes, the Ministry of Forests
operations have generated sufficient funds for investment expenditures of
about TL 400 million in 1972 and TL 500 million in 1973; however, part of
these investments are of a maintenance character (reforestation, road mainte-
nance, nursery). Additionally, almost TL 600 million equity have been accumu-
lated in the past 30 years. The Ministry of Forests also prepares financial
statements; comparable to corporations. The last available, 1970, shows a
net profit after taxes of TL 137 million which substantially exceeds the bud-
geted cash surplus for that year.  Given the strong cash generation, MF
has presently no outstanding loan obligations. A foreign loan from the Export-
Import Bank which was granted in earlier years has been repaid.
Foreign loans passed on by the Ministry of Finance are shown in
the Revolving Fund as income when they are taken down and the respective in-
vestments are included in the investment expenditures. Interest payments
and repayments of the loan are shown in the Revolving Fund as expenditures.
In the financial statements, outstanding loan amounts are shown as liabilities
and interest payments as expenses.
The three different budgets within the Ministry of Forests and its
financial statements have been summarized as well as presented in detail
below:
(a)  Ministry of Forests - The budget of the Ministry of Forestry
itself. Due to the set-up of organizations this budget is
relatively unimportant because it covers just the personnel
costs of the ministry itself. fn 1973 the budget amounts to
TL 20 million which is covered through the national budget.
(b)  Annex Budget - The Annex Budget provides for forest activities
which are of general and national nature and therefore not directly
allocable to the forest operations of an individual forestry
district. However, it is financed through the forest operations
of the various districts. The General Directorate of Forests
is in charge of the Annex Budget activities.



ALNMX 3
Page 7
(c) Revolving Fund - All forest activities related to production
are covered in the Revolving Fund. Therefore, it is by far
the largest budget within the Ministry of Forests. The
General Directorate of Forests is also in charge of the overall
coordination of the revolving fund whereby detailed execution
of the Revolving Fund activities takes place in the individual
Forestry Districts.

The MY too is in a period of transition as it passes from being
an nstitiution dominated by the goal of preserving the forest estate to one
p-3esed on all sides to increase the availability of woods It is perhaps better
D r,are tjiaL SEKA to face the transition -roam the viewpoint of good technical
lacilities (forest infrastructure) and good technical staff dispersed throughout
t.-,e nation.
It is however hampered by conservative staff in the administrative
anid policy-making positions not yet attuned to the needed changes.  Moreover,
ts..e wtidespread influence of the MP, and the political sensitivities raised
whe-, change (any change) is mentioned, inhibits the MF staff even more.
New directions have again, as in the case of SEKA, been introduced
i- the Antalya Project (see below) which should augur well for future long term
improvements as long as they are firmly applied and reinforced as the project
is implemented.
B.   SEKA
History and Organization
SEFA was established in 1934 with its plant and head office in
Izmit. It is organized as a State Economic Enterprise under Law 440. SEKA
is -ully Government owned with an authorized share capital of TL 1 billion
of which about TL 500 million are paid in. At present, SEKA is the only
major pulp and paper producer in Turkey with a total pulping capacity of
about 325,000 tons per year. Until the mid '60's, Izmit was the only SEKA
plant and it produced various kinds of pulp, paper and chemicals based on
wood and straw and principal raw materials. At that time three additional
plants (Caycuma, Aksu and Dalaman) were built and SEKA is considering
three new projects - Antaiya, Balikesir and Afyon).  The plants, the products
they produce, their capacity, raw materials and staffing are given in Table 5.
Comments on Facilities
The Izmit Plant has considerable obsolete and inefficient equipment.
Its total capacity is i20,00O t/yr of paper and it has 12 paper machines since
the pl-ant produces a wide variety of papers and different machines are neces-
sary.  The paper making equipment is generally in good mechanical and operating
condition.  'I'ere is no reason to suggest that it should all be scrapped.
T'he pulp making equipment is obsolete and produces harmful liquid and gaseous
effluents,



ANNEX 3
Page 15
The plant was built when Iznit was small. Now the plant is in a
booming industrial center. Its degrading effect on the environment must
therefore be a big factor in considering changes in the facilities.
The three modern mills of SEKA, undersized by modern standards,
have efficient and technically up-to-date equipment. The Caycuma and
Dalaman mills, however, are badly situated with respect to available raw
material and product type.
Caycuma produces industrial papers such as sackkraft and linerboard,
yet it is located in a forest region (basmudurluk) of hardwoods (good for
writing paper). Dalaman which produces printing and writing paper has forest
resources of pine that are better suited to the industrial papers produced
at Caycuma. This anomaly must be taken as a fait accompli and future ex-
pansions or modifications to these plants must try to ameliorate the situation
as much as possible.

Page 17
Caycuma and Aksu have reached design capacity and efficiency.
Dalman started in 1972 and is experiencing technical difficulties due to poor
equipment performance. SEKA feels it will overcome this within the next 12
to 24 months. In the meantime Dalaman will reach at least 40% of the capa-
city.
The Balikesir project has been temporarily shelved as discussed in
Annex 2.  Its role will be assessed in the future studies and is also
discussed in detail in Annex 6.
The Afyon project is to produce bleached pulp from reeds. It does
not appear to be a sound pulp making idea, given the availability of wood in
Turkey. There are social problems in the Afyon area, due to the political
decision to stop poppy growing and a program of alternative employment is
connected with the Government's plan for the region - thus the reluctance to
accept criticism for the Afyon project in contrast to the Balikesir project.
Further into the future, SEKA realizes that it will have other
major projects (some are discussed in Annex 6), and it would not be
surprising if investments of $500 million or more were needed in the next
decade.
SEKA Management and Staff
SEKA's six-member Board has one representative from the Ministry
of Finance, the Ministry of Industry and Technology and Labor. It also has
three members of SEKA's top management. The Board functions as a policy-
making and controlling body as well as a liaison and coordinating group
between SEKA and the Government. The influence of GOT on finance and ex-
pansion planning is felt strongly in the Company's management. The Organiza-
tion Chart is given in Figure 1.
The Managing Director and Chairman of the Board has been Aziz Gumus.
He has had extensive managerial experience and is effectively managing the
company's operations. The other members are:
Ibrahim Aldemir           Assistant Manager (Administrative)
Nahit Avci                Assistant Manager (Technical)
Hicri Sezen               Representative of Ministry of Finance
Hilmi Onat                Representative of Ministry of Industry and
Technology
Halit Cansever            Representative of Laborers.
As a State Economic Enterprise, SEKA's salaries are equal to civil
service salaries based on the Personnel Law.

Page 18
It is difficult for industrial enterprises in the public sector to
attract or to retain capable and trained staff, particularly in middle and
upper management. Considering these difficulties and the substantial ex-
pansion program SEKA has been undertaking since 1965, has strained SEKA's
resources, particularly in the middle management level, and has resulted in
frequent changes in positions. Changes in the restrictive Personnel Law
to enable State Economic Enterprises to determine compensation for middle
and upper management on a competitive basis would be highly desirable, parti-
cularly for rapidly expanding enterprises such as SEKA. It is well recognized
that such a change would have wide implications on the Turkish economy which
would have to be considered.
SEKA employs about 9,000 people. This is high, even taking into
account this figure includes operating personnel and ancillary employment
for Company-operated schools, hospitals and townships. Quality and quantity
of staff will play an ever increasing role in SEKA's future.
Financial Analysis of SEKA - Past and Future
Historical and projected financial statements have been prepared
by SEKA through 1978 which is the starting date of commercial production
of the proposed project. Due to the differences in accounting systems these
statements had to be revised.
Operations and Income Statements
Income Statements are shown in detail in Tables 6,7, and 8. They
indicate that while SEKA was only operating the almost fully depreciated
plant at Izmit, it obtained reasonable levels of profit reaching TL 117 million
before taxes in 1969. SEKA's past earnings to 1972 were also to a large
extent the result of duty free imported paper sold at domestic market prices.
After many delays and cost overruns, Caycuma and Aksu are expected
to reach full capacity utilization this year. The large Dalaman plant with
its major operating problems has an expected capacity utilization of about
50% in its second year of operation. In 1972, Dalaman lost about TL 180
million which more than offset the earnings of TL 143 million at Izmit. In
1972 on a consolidated basis, SEKA lost TL 53 million before and TL 96
million after taxes since according to Turkish tax laws, earnings and losses
cannot be consolidated for tax purposes since each establishment is considered
as a different legal and fiscal entity.
In the current year some improvement in operations is expected in
most of SEKA's plants but Dalaman as the most important plant will probably
not show substantial improvement until 1974. Once the operating difficulties
at Dalaman have been overcome, SEKA's financial performance should improve
rapidly to a satisfactory level.

Financial Situation
As for most of the enterprises in Turkey, SEKA's equity portion
is small (see Balance Sheet - Table 8). As of year end 1971, the debt/
equity ratio amounted to 75:25 after a substantial increase in equity in
1971. Half of the long-term debt of about TL 1,800 million originated
from the State Investment Bank and the other half from foreign sources.
For the Caycuma and Dalaman projects, most of the foreign loans were granted
by the European Investment Bank. The current ratio of SEKA has been about
1.1:1 during the past years. The debt/service coverage (including taxes on
income) was low in 1972 and will be low for the next two years mainly
as a result of the operating difficulties of the new plants.
SEKA's financial situation although less than satisfactory is
considered acceptable for a Bank operation, given the Government ownership
and its influence in investments and operations, as well as the established
financing mechanism through SIB.
Conclusion
SEKA is in a period of transition. From being a one plant, over-
staffed organization with inefficient and obsolete equipment, it has moved
rapidly into the modern -era with its three new plants. They all have their
difficulties some of which, like size and location, are there to stay. Now
SEKA faces a future where capacity expansion will be the order of the day.
Its resources will be stretched to the utmost. Experienced staff
who are willing to move to new locations will be hard to find at the low
salary levels. The limited financial resources will mean constant inter-
vention by the owner - the State.
Clearly SEKA will need major modifications of its systems and
organization to deal with its new situation. The attraction of good personnel
will remain a chronic difficulty until the salary structure is changed. While
this will not be tackled specifically, it is expected that new directions will
be introduced by the Antalya Project (see Chapter 11) but they will only be
the start of what will have to be a constant renewal effort.

C.   Organization of the Sawmill Industry
About 90% of the domestic market needs are met by private sector
mills and the balance of State mills operated by the Ministry of Forestry.
Sawnwood production facilities in the private sector are usually
owned and operated by family concerns. There are over 5,000 private sawmills
with a productive capacity of about six million m3/A of roundwood. This repre-
sents an average mill size of about 1,200 m3/A, with only two mills having a
capacity exceeding 20,000 m3 per year.
Most of the private sector mills are of unsophisticated design and
their operations are housed in buildings of light and cheap construction
usually with dirt floors, open sides and poor lighting. The principal equip-
ment component is a band headsaw of small capacity.  The carriage on which
the log is fed to the saw is usually manually operated including the energy
needed to feed the log to the saw. A simple resaw is the second item of equip-
ment used to trim and resaw cants to boards and dimension stock as required.
Prices for equipment are relatively low, and investment of 50,000 to 100,000 TL
would be required to equip a small mill of 5,000 m3 annual roundwood input.
The private sector wood industries are generally located near
centers of consumption, e.g., Ankara and Istanbul, whereas in developed
countries this is not usually the case. Economic justification for this
is that there is high value in wood by-products (sawdust for fuel, sawlogs
trimmed on 3 sides and used for squatter housing, fences, etc.)
Private sawmills purchase logs at public auctiois held at receiving
depots. The Ministry of Forestry sets a minimum bid price. In addition to
the bid price, the successful buyer must pay the various State, Provincial
and local taxes which amount to 24% of the purchase price.
Small private sawmills are labor intensive because of high costs
of equipment and logs. Recovery rates, volume of lumber sold in relation to
volume to logs purchased, were reported to be in the order of a perhaps
overstated high of 70 to 75%. In general, sawnwood quality in public sector
mills is above the private sector. Average prices received are higher. Re-
coveries are lower, about 65% versus the 70-75% figure for the private
sector. This is attributable to the full dimension cutting practices and
a greater concentration on grade.
Manufacturing costs in public mills are estimated at two to three
times higher than in the private sector. This is due to:



AbNNEX 3
Page 26
- nominal recovery rates
- higher wage scales
- higher overhead costs
Parts of these costs are recovered in higher selling prices. Log
cost dominates in total production cost. In normal times it is between 80 and
90% of all conversion costs.
The small private owner-manager usually handles sales activities as
incidental to his general management functions.
A large segment of private sector mills old and using low cost
equipment, are cottage industry. They often operate on an intermittent basis
depending upon log supplies, local market needs, prices and weather conditions.
Other mills are integrated with joinery factories and are geared to their
needs. Other mills are custom operators and provide only a sawing service
to customers at a "per cubic meter" price. Customers provide the logs.
There are no mandatory sawnwood grading rules as in State operations.
This is part of the reason why most sales are made at mill sites. Some of
the large private mills have adopted grading procedures. Grades and the
finishing of joinery grades is carried out in the joinery plants. Kiln
drying is not common, though at least one moderately large private sector
mill has it.
The moisture content of shipped sawnwood is not given much attention,
and varies largely due to market conditions. The long period during which logs
and sawnwood are in storage between time of log harvesting and of sawnwood sales
allows the wood to approach air-dry conditions. When markets are strong this
natural control does not exist.
Institution-building aspects of the Project
From the beginning of project identification, the Government insisted
that a branch of the Ministry of Forestry would operate the forestry part,
and that a subsidiary of SEKA, the pulp and paper part.  The international
institutions resisted this concept because of the importance they attached to
the project parts being fully integrated, while noting the difficulty of
coordinating SEKA and the Ministry of Forestry, two semi-autonomous agencies.



ANNEX 3
Page 27
The international institutions felt these problems would be overcome
if a new private pulp and paper company was formed with possible concessionary
rights to logging in the Antalya forests. A competitive spur to the existing
system they thought would force a change in obsolete ways.
The Government despite this pressure and a recognition of failure
stuck to its insistence that improvement be mainly from within. Foreign
participation in the equity and permanent management of the operation was
refused outright. The Government argued SEKA and the Ministry of Forestry
were vital. Unfair competition to them (as would be the case of a company
not bound by the Civil Service Code for salary levels as was SEKA) could not
be supported.
These were felt to be convincing arguments, which became more
apparent as the feasibility study progressed and the inherent - if hidden -
ability of SEKA and the Ministry of Forestry staff became apparent.

THE FORESTRY PROJECT
Introduction
This chapter deals with the forestry aspects of the Antalya Forest
Util:Lzation Project. The first part reviews the Antalya basmudurluk resource
base: the species grown, extent of forested area, standing volumes, log
quality and the quantity of wood that can be cut annually without reducing
the wood capital. This is followed by a discussion on the way the forest
will be mamaged to achieve by 1979 a felling programme of 790,000 m3 annually
using clear felling techniques and replacement with improved, man-made
plantations. The third section examines the future demand for wood grown in
Antalya, and indicates the additional amounts that will be required from the
Isparta, Adana and Mersin basmudurluks to satisfy SEKA's requirements. The
method of logging and transportation is also described. Finally the Annex
deals with organization and management, manpower requirements and method of
implementation. In Annexes 10 and 13 capital and operational costs are dealt
with in detail.
Investment needs for the forestry project from 1973-77 are TL 281
million with a foreign exchange component of US$8.0 million. During this
period forestry project operations would comprise the:
-    construction of some 2,000 km of forest roads and a bridge;
-    maintenance of some 25,000 km of forest roads;
-    planting of over 38,000 ha of cut-over or degraded forest;
-    production of 2.21 million m3 of logs, 4.6 million steres
of fuelwood and 5,560 tons of resin, and
-    protection of the forest estate from fire, disease and erosion.
Forest Resources of the Antalya Basmudurluk
Species
The forests are formed by coniferous species occuring in pure stands.
However, some mixed coniferous stands do exist in relatively inaccessible
locations at high elevations.  Neither of the hardwood species (2uercus so,:)
or Plantanus orientalis) occur in significant quantities or are of commercial
importance.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
Pinus brutia (kilzilcam) is the most important species found from
sea level to 1,100 m asl. The other major commercial species in declining
order of occurrence are Pinus nigra (karacam), Abies cilicia (goknar), and
:luni2erus sDp. (ardic). These are all found at higher elevations and generally
orm pure stands.  Two other conifers, Pinus pinea (fistikcam) and Cypressus
s_mpervirens var horizontalis (servic) also grow in small areas but are of
minor commercial importance.

Allowable Annual Cut (AAC) 1/
One of the most important requirements for the calculation of
Allowable Annual Cut is a sound prediction of growth and yield. This data
is usually obtained through a system of permanent sample plots. At present
no such plots exist in Antalya and will have to be established in order to
prepare a better prediction of AAC in the future. In the absence of this
information, the following data taken from temporary sample plots was used
to calculate AAC for P. brutia and P. nigra assuming average growing sites.
|   Tne maximum annual amount of wood which may be harvested from a given
area on. the basis of sustained yield (equal or increasing yields in
perpetuity).

Future Inventories
The first major forest inventory of Antalya was carried out in 1964
and was designed to obtain gross overbark volumes. A second major re-inven-
tory for the area is scheduled for 1973/74, and is designed to determine net
merchantable under bark volumes. In addition, it will define industrial log
products in terms of dimension and quality. The new forest management plans
to be prepared by the implementation team will be based on the new inventory
data.
Forest Management
Present System
In 1964, the Uniform Shelterwood System 1/ was introduced and the
region divided into 203 series. Eacn has its own management plan and is
managed as a separate entity. The AAC is calculated for each series
separately, and the age class method of management is stipulated. This
method requires that the oldest age classes must be cut first. The present
<Gf The opening of the canopy of a mature forest on a uniform basis for
regeneration purposes to obtain an even-aged, new crop.



ANNEX 4
Page 9
system of management has no basic requirements and neither is met in Antalya.
There should be an even distribution of age classes in each series and road
access to all 203 series. By 1971, only 86 series had been logged. Further-
more, the annual cut included not only final regeneration fellings (60%) but
also "thinnings" (40%) from large overmature forests. The practice of thinn-
ing overmature stands is of questionable silvicultural value as the growth
response of remaining trees will be slight. Very little flexibility is
provided on this approach to forest management.
It has been recent practice to rely on natural regeneration of
the forests. This method has not been very successful, particularly with
P. brutia which demands plenty of light. Many of the regeneration blocks
have failed to regenerate and now have to be planted. Valuable time has
been lost between felling and re-stocking adding to the length of the rota-
tion and increasing the cost of establishment because of heavy undergrowth
development. Where natural regeneration is successful it is in any case
usually necessary to plant up to about 20% of the area.
Proposed Approach
The following principles of forest management were recommended as
a basis for implementation by the Consultants. These principles are fully
supported by the Bank and have been accepted by the MF.
-    to manage the forest on a sustained yield basis;
-    to maximixe yield by setting a rotation age for each species
such that the optimum production of net merchantable under
bark volume per unit area is achieved;
-    to achieve the objective of optimum productivity for all
forest land through liquidation, as soon as possible, of
all overmature or understocked stands, consistent with the
sustained yield constraint, and through immediate restocking
of cut-over areas with young, healthy seedlings;
-    to exclude from production those areas of forest set aside
for National Parks and roadside amenity belts or in which
successful regeneration cannot be assured;
-    to maintain the existing forested area through increased
measures of protection;
-    to expand the productive forest area by recovery of abandoned
encroached lands and by afforesting open and degraded
forest areas;
-    to distribute forest operations as widely as necessary through-
out the region to meet the needs of the forest population;



ANNEX 4
Page 10
-    to draw the balance between economic and ecological conservation
in managing the forests;. and
to introduce gradually any shift in management philosophy
necessary to achieve the principles.
Cut over areas will be reforested by:
(a)  Natural regeneration in the high elevation P. brutia and P. nigra
areas;
(b)  Seedin  in the rocky P. brutia and Cedrus areas; and
(c) Planting in the remaining areas which will form the bulk
of the reforestation program.
Afforestation
Unlike final felling and reforestation, afforestation in Turkey is
the responsibility of the General Directorate of Afforestation and Erosion
Control (GDA). The GDA operates within the forest estate on areas with 10%
or less crown cover including the maquis areas. In Antalya the total area of
degraded forest and other areas, within which.the,GDA may operate, is some
688,000 ha,  The preliminary investigation indicated that of this 88,000 ha
is potentially plantable land. Incendiarism is still common in the region
done by irate villagers to new plantations, particularly in higniy populated
areas where there is conflict of ownership and crop and pressure for grazing
land. The GDA has eliminated those areas where such danger exists.
The potential for expansion of the productive forest area through
afforestation has enabled an increase in the AAC. The increasing contribu-
tion of thinning yields.to the AAC towards the end of the rotation permits
an earlier liquidation of the overmature forest areas. There are, however,
some limitations to the extent of early afforestation. If a continuing
sustained yield, either at the present or an increased level, is to be assured,
steps must be taken to balance the distribution of age classes in the second
rotation. The present overmature and understocked nature of the forest necessi-
tates fairly extensive logging for a given volume of wood; however as these
forests are liquidated and the proportion 'of younger stands to be logged in-
creases, the required logging area will decrease.
The GDA would like .to increase the annual afforestation for Antalya
to as high as 10,000 ha/A. However, the present annual reforestation area is
over 4,000 ha and in 1978 will be over 9,000 ha. At full AAC production, this
will go as high as 9,500 ha. An additional 10,000 ha of afforestation would
put the regeneration program beyond the physical capabilities of the regional
staff even if recruitment and training were instituted immediately.
Details of the annual areas to be regenerated (reforestation alid
afforestation) are given in Table 15.  It should be possible to build up to a
program of 1 ,000 ha a year from 1978 onwards but it is a large program by any
standards.



ANNEX 4
Page 11
Thinning
The practice of supplying 40% of the AAC from thinning overmature
stands will be stopped as the silvicultural response is usually not worth the
effort. Instead, thinning will be done in the natural stands less than 40
years old where stocking control is very important to proper development.
Details of the annual areas to be thinned are contained in Table 15.

Forest Protection
The forests have to be protected from fire, encroachment, erosion,
insect and disease. This work is carried out by isletme and bolge staff.
Regular forest and plantation guard patrols are maintained for this purpose.
Fire. Most of the destructive forest fires in Antalya take place
at lower elevations in P. brutia stands. Forest fires tend to induce heavy
undergrowth development of marquis vegetation which is excellent for grazing.
These areas of destructive fires recorded over the past few years range from
about 150 ha in 1966 to 2,800 ha in 1969. The total area effected from
1964-71 was over 8,500 ha. The fire danger season extends for 5 months from



ANNEX 4
Page 12
May to October. During this period 47 look-out points are manned 24 hours a
day and are connected by radio and/or telephone to ranger, isletme and/or
basmudurIuk HQ. Three new look-out points will be built during the project
implementation. Stand-by crews will be provided with a radio equipped jeep
pick-up truck. Portable fiberglass water tanks, of about 650 to 900 liter
caapcity, and gasoline motor pump units, capable of being mounted on the back
of a pick-up truck, will be obtained for each district to facilitate fire
suppression.
There are relatively few firebreaks established in the indigenous
forest. It is the policy to create and maintain clean-weeded 10 m wide
firebreaks in new plantation areas at the boundaries of each compartment.
This practice will be continued. In addition, pruning of lower branches to
a height of 2 meters will be done at the edges of all firebreaks and roadsides
in stands 15 years and older as a fire prevention measure. Pruning will be
done on trees for 10 m into the stand on each side of the break or road.
Pruning will be carried out at the sides of all new roads whether in planta-
tions or indigenous forest. It is particularly important in the younger
stands. All firebreaks are to be weeded once a year prior to the dry season
and before grass seeding takes place. Stand-by crews will be able to cope
with all of the pruning work and some of the firebreak cultivation. Details
of the area of firebreak tending are included in Table 16.
As the main cause of fires in incendiarism, immediate short term
protection improvement involves increased patrolling of the forest, parti-
cularly in known high hazard areas. However, it is anticipated that the
development of the project will provide more work on a regular basis for
the villagers, inject more money into their economy and provide an opportunity
to improve their subsistence level of agriculture. Part of this improvement
should be a reduction in the numbers of the present poor variety of goats
with a change to a more productive species. The resultant reduction in
demand for grazing land and improved local economic conditions should signi-
ficantly reduce the problem of incendiarism.
Encroachment. During the period 1964-71, the recorded area of
illegal encroachment of forest land amounted to some 2,300 ha. The actual area
may well have been larger. The immediate answer to the problem lies in more
effective patrols. Concurrently, the local people will be made aware of the
importance of the forest. This can be achieved only through improved liaison
with the people by local forest staff.
Apart from preventing further losses, every effort will be made to
reclaim land abandoned by farmers. Such land, having been cultivated for
years, is ideally suited for natural or artificial regeneration since it is
free of persistent woody vegetation. These areas are to he fenced and actively
managed for forest uses as soon as they become available.



ANNEX 4
Page 13
Insects and Disease. Damage from insects and fungi is not a ser-
ious problem in Antalya, however, there is an insect control unit in the
region with adequate staff to spray some 10,000 ha/year. As a proportion of
logs will be delivered to the mill with bark on there will be a need to monitor
the bark beetle population. No problems should arise if the bark is removed
within 6 weeks of felliing.  In the event of a major epidemic (there has onl'j
been one in Turkey in the Black Sea region), a reserve fund is held centrally
in Ankara to provide for emergency control measures.
Soil Conservation and Erosion Control. The run-off effect, after
precipitation, particularly in areas of intensive agricultural activity at
higher elevations, can be serious in terms of erosion. Soil engineering
will be undertaken wherever necessary in forestry operations. The need for
conservation measures exist in most areas where felling takes place, even
where natural regeneration and seeding are to be used. it is estimated that
in 1% of operating areas, special measures, over and above normal soil
engineering, will be necessary to prevent gully erosion. Details of areas
involved are included in Table 16.
Forest Research
There is pressing need to expand research activities in Antalya to
support this project. In particular, the following topics are to be given
high priority during project implementation.
-    tree breeding - the establishment of seedling or clonal seed
orchards on a national level to obtain genetically improved
planting stock;
-    nursery trials - improvement in techniques;
-    seed handling technology;
-    site classification;
-    establishment trials - land preparation and subsequent
cultivation and planting replacement;
-    species trials - particularly exotics such as
Pinus pinaster, P. radiata, Eucalyptus camaidulensis,
tereticornis, grandis and glubulus;
-    provenance trials;
-    thinning trials;
-    fertilizat ? on trials;
-    growth and yield studies; and
-    log production studies,



ANNEX 4
Page 14

be expanded from 8 to 17 professionals with technical support also provided.

Demand for Forest Products
Present Demand
As noted earlier, the forest legislation of Turkey provides that
all villagers living within 10 km of the forests have the right to certain
volumes of forest products for their own or community use. In addition,
there is provision for the supply of logs for building purposes in the event
of a disaster, e.g. an earthquake. The MF also retains certain forest



ALXNEX 4
Page 15
products for official use in forestry activities. All such demands on the
forest have priority and are provided at special low prices. These priority
demands are referred to as Statutory Requirements.
Within the Antalya basmu,1urluks there are over 450 small sawmills
(including joinery shops) and wooden box plants which purchase required log
supplies at the MOF log auctions. The state operates one sizeable box plant
at Antalya which is supplied with logs through an agreement. The manufactur-
ing facilities provide for regional needs of construction lumber and wooden
boxes. While individually the plants are not of significant size, collectivell
they provide employment for many people and also meet specific local needs.
The log demands for this sector of the regional economy are referred to as
"Regional Requirements."
Of the industrial log volume, 55% is sold to other basmudurluks.
Included in these sales are mine props and railway ties for use by state
enterprises elsewhere in Turkey. The logs that are shipped outside the
region in unmanufactured form are referred to as "Sales to Other Regions."
Sales to state organizations are arranged through agreements with the General
Directorate of Forestry in Ankara and sales for privately owned manufacturing
plants are made through MF public auctions.
The recorded sales of logs from Antalya in 1971 are shown on the
next page.

Forest Production
Logging and Transportation
Felling. The felling is presently a manual operation (hand saws
and axes) with few power chain saws in use. Similarly limbing and cutting
into log lengths is carried out manually. The maximum length of SEKA's
sawlogs will be 5 m as the skidding and transportation facilities discussed
below would not be able to cope with longer logs. Pulpwood will be cut to
1 meter lengths. Wide scale use of power saws would increase productivity
by some 20%, reduce the work effort and eventually provide increased income
to the workers. Provision is made in the project for the purchase of power
saws for sale to individuals. Facilities for maintenance and repair will
be provided by the GDF or Cooperatives. Other alternative mechanical felling
methods are deemed impractical because of terrain limitations.
Barking. Logs are currently barked in the forest with hand tools
prior to skidding. This reduced the damage by bark beetles and borers to a
minimum and is cheaper than on-site mechanical systems. However, if labor
becomes scarce, it will be necessary for a portion of the production to be
barked by SEKA at mill and provisions are being made to handle this eventua-
lity. Logs transported to log depots for public sale will continue to be
debarked manually in the forest.



ANNEX 4
Page 20
Skidding. Skidding or delivery of logs to roadside is accomplished
by mmue and oxen. If the terrain prohibits animals then logs are manhandled or
tahro-m or pushed to roadside. Mechanical skidding methods using wheeled or tracked
type surface vehicles and cable systems will not be used except on a pilot
basis to gain experience and train machine operators. Terrain obstacles
and steep slopes restrict the potential use of surface vehicles to a few
forest areas, and low stand volumes/ha plus small operating blocks would
require frequent relocation of a cable system resulting in high set-up and
moving costs.
Transportation. Logs for public sale are transported from roadside
to forest depots wnere they are sorted, stored and sold. Virtually all log
transport takes place in 3-5 ton trucks (average load 12.5 m3) which for the
most part are privately owned.  Loading and unloading are both manual opera-
tions carried out by men hired by the truck driver.  In view of the manual
nature of the work, employment in the transportation operation is high and,
at the present time in Antalya, is estimated to involve in the order of
7,000 people on a part-time basis. Road improvements and more powerful trucks
will improve transportation costs and improvements will be made in loading
techniques. However, the use of special machines for loading is not at
present deemed to be economic. Sawlogs required by SEKA will be collected
from log depots barked or unbarked. Pulpwood will be stacked by species at
roadside by the MF. Responsibility for transportation to mill site of
sawlogs and pulpwood will be SEKA's.  At full production there will be one
log truck arriving at SEKA's mill site every 3 minutes. Improvements in the
state highways will be required to cope with this volume.  The remaining log
production will continue to be transported to log depots at the MF's cost.
Transportation of fuelwood is presently the buyer's responsibility. The MF
has fuelwood piled at roadside, and sells it at that point.
Most logging work is done by some form of a sub-contract or job
basis. Labor input records are practically non-existant. Based on produc-
tivity data and assuming an individual works between 75 and 138 days annually,
it is estimated that about 7,400 are employed annually on log production
work.
Thus, the logging and construction activities will continue to be
labor intensive. However, it is anticipated that this will change gradually
as younger villagers enter the labor force and wages increase.
The importance of competent field supervisors who are thoroughly
familiar with production methods and techniques must be emphasized. The
successful development of a skilled labor force and implementation of improved
techniques is a continuous activity in which the field supervisors play a key
role.



ANEX 4
Page 21
Careful detailed planning and scheduling are essential to make
maximum use of equipment and to provide year round employment for skilled
machine operators and production workers. This will also assist in the
development of a skilled labor force and contribute to more efficient
operations.
Log Depots
The system of public auction works well with the exception that
it requires storage of logs for long periods with subsequent loss in grade.
Within the Antalya basmudurluk there are 29 log depots, distributed among
the 14 isletmes. Each depot handles only logs produced in the isletme. The
number per isletme varies from I to 5. All main depots are located at
points on or near good highways so they are readily accessible for subsequent
transport. Steps to reduce the number of depots will be taken once the quan-
tity of logs sold directly has increased.
Roadside and depot log inventories will fluctuate during the year
and also vary from year to year. However, recent year-end inventories
represent between 3 and 6 months supply.
Roads
Roads are built to a single standard and only those sections in
use are maintained. By machine, construction costs are about TL 31,000/km;
by hand, TL 51,000/km. Road construction methods are generally satisfactory.
In the future, roads will be constructed to two standards: main haul roads
and feeder roads. According to the following specifications:
Main Roads    Feeder Roads
Width of running surface                            4.0 m         3.5 m
Maximum favorable grade                              12 %          12 %
Maximum adverse grade                                 6 %           6 %
Minimum curve radius                                 20 m          15 m
These will allow loaded trucks speeds of             25 km/hr      15 km/hr
By 1979, 475 km of main. roads and a bridge over the Koprucay River will be
constructed and paid for out of the capital budget. Secondary roads paid
for out of operating expenses are required at a rate of some 300 km/year.
Details of distances required are contained in Table 15. A substantial
road maintenance program starting with some 4,000 km in 1973 and building
up to over 10,000 km annually by 1991 will also be undertaken. The quality
of maintenance will be improved.



ANNEX 4
Page 22
To carry out this program, substantial additional equipment as
well as replacement equipment (including spare parts for both) is required.
At a total cost over 5 years (1973-77) of TL 75 million, it is the largest
single item in the forestry project's capital cost requirement.
No difficulties are expected in being able to achieve this road
construction and maintenance program since the basmudurluk is already able
to construct 300 km annually with out of date and insufficient equipment.
Feeder road construction costs in other parts of the world vary between
TL 14,000 and TL 84,000 per km thus Turkish costs are very competitive.
Other Facilities
Some additional office space, housing, equipment and maintenance
shops and other miscellaneous buildings will be built and have been included
in domestic capital costs.   Forest guards' housing has been treated as an
operational cost.
Buildings. The Central Workshops in Antalya are well run and can
undertake practically all the major equipment repairs required. However,
further equipment will be obtained through the project to enable workshops
to handle all the additional machinery being purchased. Eight mobile,
repair pick-ups will be purchased for operation in the Divisions.
Powersaw supply and repair shops could be incorporated into the
isletme shop structure or handled on a private basis.  The additions,
expansions and modifications shown below will adequately cover requirements
for construction during the project.

Detailed estimates of manpower requirements were prepared by the consultants
and the summary figures shown above, bearing in mind the work program, are
thought to reflect needs satisfactorily. By 1979 the forestry project will
provide full time employment for an additional 4,300 persons in the Antalya
Forest basmudurluk. The most significant increase in labor requirements is
the skilled category. The organization of a training program to ensure the
present workers fill this need will be one of the main tasks of the project
implementation team to be described later. About 11,000 man-years of labor
will be required at full production. However, the actual number employed
will be in the order of 15,000 as many workers will only work on a part-time
basis. Production operators can be scheduled on a year round basis whereas
forestry work, and particularly tree planting, is more seasonal.
Salaries
Salary levels are based on formal training, number of years service
and to some degree, level of responsibility. Fringe benefits apply to all
salaried employees. Gross income including fringe benefits and supplements
range from TL 8,000/year (US$570) for a laborer to TL 105,000/year (US$7,500)
for the regional Director.



ANNEX 4
Page 26
Forest Service Staff salaries are generally substantially below
tnose paid by the private sector to persons bearing similar responsibilities.
Steps to improve this situation will be required in the future.

Proposed Organization
The basic system of basmudurluks, isletmes and bolge organizations
described in Annex 3 permits rational, practical forest development and no
change in this structure is required. However, realignment of functions
within these units and some rationalization of unit areas and boundaries
were recommended by the Consultants and are currently being considered by
the MOF.  The specific changes proposed by Sandwell listed below will be
reviewed during implementation.
-    reduce isletmes from 14 to 9;
- - retain two example (Ornek) isletmes for pilot operations,
but hand over management and planning to the research group,
(routine production, road construction and maintenance, etc.
would be taken over by the appropriate adjacent regular
isletmes);
-    Reduce bolges from 75 to 36;
-    establish a major production group in both basmudurluk and
isletme organizations. All production activities would be
undertaken by the isletme production staff - none by the
bolge staff;
-    establish major forest management and silviculture groups in
both regional and Isletme organizations. All forest management
work would be undertaken by the enlarged bolges;



ANNEX 4
Page 29
-    join all accounting, statistical and other support activities
into major administration groups at basmudurluk and isletme
level (these groups would undertake all routine administrative
office procedures and reduce the paper work burden that all
senior officers now have to handle, thereby permitting the
skilled axperienced responsible forest officers to devote more
time on supervision control and ?launing);
-    establish a regional planning team to coordinate plans prepared
by specialist sections, develop rational practical plans for
the region and provide liaison with the MF and GDF planning
teams in Ankara; and
introduce control systems including cost accounting, production
and forestry activity statistics, equipment utilization and cost
analysis and labor use amd productivity records.
The appraisal mission agrees with the proposals put forth by the
consultants but realizes the need to be flexible on the actual number of
organizational units finally selected.

Implementation
At the implementation of this plan requires a substantial amount
of work in addition to that required to conduct normal operations, a
Turkish Implementation Team will be established. The team will require two
experienced senior foresters responsible for silvicultur.al and forest
production activities, respectively, plus supporting staff.  Total man month
requirements including support staff are estimated to be 630 over a 6 year
period.  Vehicles, office space and equipment will be provided for the team.
To assist the Turkish Team and zo provide additional breadth of
experience will require the services of a foreign forestry consultancy team,,
preferably from Forestal. Three Consultantss are required intermittently over
6 years for a total of some 100 man months; one covering production and
administration and the others silviculture and forest management.  They would
be assisted from time to time by specialists for an estimated 15 man months.
Shown below is the estimated domestic and foreign costs required
from 1973-79 to cover the expenses of the combined Turkish-Consultants
Implementation Team.  Provisions will be made by the project for financing
the first 5 years expenses.

The implementation team will be concerned with the three levels of
planning prescribed below:
Planning Level           Term Period    Relative Scope of Planning Detail
1. Management Plan       Long term -      For whole basmudurluks.  State

isletme and bolge.
Levels 2 and 3 include both operating and development plans; the former deals
with forestry objectives and quantitative production targets, the latter
covers all aspects of those supporting operations, such as road building and
maintenance necessary to achieve the forestry objectives.
This would do away with the present practice of producing a
management plan for each of 203 series in the basmudurluk but would not mean
a change in the number of series. These are basic units of management and
statistical information and must be preserved.



ANNEX 4
Page 35
The proposed 1973 re-inventory provides an excellent opportunity
to establish a very sound basis for planning. Detailed preparation of new
management plans will be delayed until the 1973 results are available. In
the meantime, a synthesis of the information contained in Consultant's
report should be adequate for interim planning.
(A detailed wood supply contract is currently being drawn up in
Turkey between MF, the supplier and SEKA, the user on the lines described
in Annex 3).
Preparation for this project by the Antalya Basmudurluk Staff is
already well in hand and there is an awareness of the changes needed in
forest management, silviculture, logging and administration of the forest
estate to achieve the objective of establishing a fully integrated industrial
complex based on the optimum use of Antalya's wood resources. Change will
have to be based on sound principles, study and careful implementation as
the project develops and needs become more apparent. The key to the project
is the creation of the Turkey-Consultants Implementation Team which will be
operating by mid-1974.
