ANNEX 5
Page I
THE INDUSTRIAL PROJECT
Introduction
The intensive industrial utilization of the lightly-utilized
forests of the Antalya region has been the subject of study for many years.
Tnis previous work led to a UNDP-financed detailed feasibility study in 1972
and early 1973 of such utilization by Sandwell and Company, Ltd., Consulting
Engineers of Vancouver, Canada.  Sandwell was chosen for this work on the
basis of previous experi.ence and performance in similar detailed studies in
many parts of the world.
SEKA, on its part, has been seeking a basis for the establishment
of a truly integrated forest industries complex, which it does not enjoy at
its existing four pulp and paper mills. These mills must obtain pulpwood
from wherever in Turkey it may be available, and at times they must resort
to pulpwood imports in order to maintain operations.
This would be the first plant in Turkey to utilize the well-esta-
blished principle of forest industries integration at a single site, with
the resulting economy of utilizing the residues of mechanical wood process-
ing (sawnwood, veneer, plywood) in the manufacture of woodpulp and paper.
The principle also permits the determination at the point of entry of the
best use to be made of each log or part thereof. Only the higher grades of
sawnwood normally command a satisfactory price, so that low-grade logs are
more economically converted to woodpulp and paper. The Plant would contain
a sawmill, a pulp mill and a paper mill. The suggested Plant Layout is shown
at the end of this Annex (World Bank 7848). A process flow diagram is also
shown there (World Bank 7847).
Suitability of Wood Species
As noted in Annex 4, over 80% of the wood supply consists of pinus
brutia, a pine native to Antalya. There are four minor species that are also
coniferous. There are no significant broadleaves species in Antalya.
Pinus brutia, in the realm of sawnwood, is suitable only for con-
struction lumber. It suffers from brittleness, poor nailing characteristics,
warping, it contains many knots, and it bleeds pitch. Nonetheless, it is
widely used in Turkey, air-dried, in construction. Warping and pitch bleed-
ing might be solved by proper kiln drying as has been done with similar
southern United States pines, although kilr. drying is not included in the
project. Lebanon cedar (6% of the volume) is already well accepted in
joinery and furaiture manufacture.
There are insufficient logs of adequate quality and diameter to
consider veneer and plywood manufacture.



ANNEX 5
Page 2
3roadly speaking, kraft pulps produced from coniferous species
fall into two strength qualify classifications. The first of these,
t.ypifled by Scandanavian pulps, are high in bursting and tensile strength
but somewhat low in tearing strength. The second, typified by Douglas fir
plps from the west coast of North America, are low in bursting and tensile
s5trengt  but high in tearing strength.  Laboratory tests indicate that pirus
br-ia --would falA into the latter category.  This raises doubts about the
sui-,ability of pinus brutia in the manufacture of sackkraft meeting inter-
national spec-ifications.  Such paper has rigorous strength requirements;
othe7wise the sacks have a high incidence of breakage in use.  It is under-
stood, however, that SEKA does not intend to -^oroduce sack paper meeting
interrational specifications, and intends to make up any strength deficiencies
by using more paper in each sack than would be required with first-quality
sackkraft.  This practice is also followed by some Southern United States
paper manufacturers that have their own sack plants; the pines grown in the
area tend to have strength problems similar to that of pinus brutia.
Strength requirements for linerboard are less rigorous than for
sacjkkraft. No problems are expected in meeting linerboard specifications
utilizing pinus brutia.
TEhe minor species are so small a part of the pulpwood supply that
they are not expected to have any significant effect upon the papermaking
characteristics of the pulp produced.
Description of Sawmill
The design and operating concepts incorporated in the proposed
sawmill are based on an analysis of the available resources, the logistics
of log supply, the requirements of the sawnwood market and the manufactur-
ing standards applicable in the market. The sawmill capacity 1/ was esta-
blished by limiting it to process only those logs of sawlog quality exceed-
ing 20 cn top diameter and 2.50 to 5.0 meters in length. The current
practice in small sawmills in Turkey of recovering small sections of short
length lumber from slabs and edgings would not be followed because of the
relatively low sales value and high manufacturing costs of such small
sections. The slabs and edgings would be chipped for use as pulpwood,
which is considered to be a higher order of utilization.
Sawlogs over 88 cm in diameter would be hand-barked in the woods.
Sawlogs would be bucked to a maximum length of 5.0 meters in the woods.
Bucking of logs at the sawmill would be preferred in order to realize the
hignLest possible sales return and would be examined in the future.  Sawlogs
would be sorted in the forest by species and be delivered by trucks equipped
wi t  bunks to permit unlioading at the sawmill by front end loader.  All
sawlogs would be weighed at the log yard entrance and random samples would
v/  Input:  340,000 M3/yr
Output: t82,000 m3/yr



ANINEX 5
Page 3
be scaled to develop a weight-log volume relationship for receiving, sawmill
production and accounting purposes. Logs would be segregated by species
and inito two diameter classifications in order to minimize subsequent
sawnwood sorting and permit more efficient sawmill and sales operations.
Stored logs would be protected against checking, staining, insect damage
and fire by a system of irrigation type sprinklers. An inventory of up
to three months sawlogs in the mill yard would be provided to average out
short term supply fluctuations which could limit the log supply during these
periods of the year when road conditions or competition from other industries
for truck transport could restrict delivery.
Sawlogs 88 cm and smaller in diameter would be fed through a
mechanical debarker as the first processing step. Bark would be hogged and
fed to the power boiler as fuel.  The primary breakdown equipment best
suited to the available log supply would be a conventional bandmill and
carriage in order to maximize recovery of grade and volume. Since the
annual sawlog volume allocated for lumber manufacture can be processed by
a single bandmill and carriage headrig, adequately supported by secondary
breakdown and remanufacturing capacity, this basic equipment arrangement
best suits the project requirements.
The single-cut bandmill and no-man carriage headrig would be followed
by a Quad Bandmill equipped with line bar and hold down rolls, a double feed
bed horizontal resaw, two resaws, two adjustable pre-set four saw edgers,
and a selective automatic trimmer.
The sawmill would process one species at a time, and the sawnwood,
small timbers, and flitches produced would be manually sorted by thickness
and length, and in certain instances by moisture content and grade, into
piles made ready for air drying by the interleaving of strips of wood.
Completed loads of sawnwood would be taken by fork lift to the air-drying
yard, where they would be suitably oriented to the prevailing wind to provide
the best drying conditions. The loads would be lock piled three loads high
by six loads wide and covered to restrain the top layers of sawnwood as well
as to protect the top of the loads from direct exposure to the sun.
Air dry loads of sawnwood would be drawn from storage as called for
by sales requirements and delivered by fork lift to the dry sorter plant.
There the sawnwood would be defect-trimmed, graded, marked, double end
trimmed and sorted by final grade and length into loads ready for shipment
by truck or temporary storage in the sales storage area, where it would be
protected against the weather by covers similar to those described above.
Description of Pulpwood Preparation
The pulpwood storage yard would be adequate to store a three months
supply to insure a wood supply during those periods of the year when road con-
ditions or competition from seasonal industries for truck transport limits



ANNEX 5
Page 4
deliveries.  All pulpwood would be weighed at the log yard entrance and
random samples would be scaled to develop a weight-volume relationship for
receiving, wood chip production and accounting purposes. Pulpwood would
normally be delivered bark-on in one meter lengths, 8 cm and larger in
dianeter segregated by species, and stored in ranked piles in the yard.
The wood preparation plant would be sized to process up to
!t7?9inn r!3/A of pulpwood as reouired when the plant produces Kraft liner-
board at design capacity.
To the extent possible, the wood preparation plant's infeed
conveyor would be filled directly from incoming trucks to minimize handling
costs. The minor species would be fed separately from pinus brutia. Bark
would be removed in the same mechanical debarker used for the sawmill. Pro-
vision would be made for the offside bandsaw breakdown of pulpwood larger
in diameter than the chipper can handle. A metal detector would be provided
before the chipper for protection against imbedded metal in the pulpwood.
Chips would be conveyed pneumatically to paved open storage and reclaim
areas which would permit the segregated storage of pinus brutia chips, minor
species chips, and sawdust for a maximum of one month. The storage piles
would permit effective rotation of inventory in order to minimize chip
deterioration. Provision would be made so that a controlled blend of 79%
pinus brutia chips, 15% minor species chips, and 6% mixed species sawdust
could be withdrawn from storage. This blend of chips and sawdust would then
be screened to remove oversize chips, reduce the size of these chips, and
returni them to the system. This final blend is then ready for conveying
to a surge bin in the pulp mill.
Description of Pulp Mill
The process to be used would be the standard kraft (also called
sulpiiate) process with a complete chemical recovery system. This process
would provide maximum pulp strength properties for the paper and board to
be made, together with the economies and pollution abatement aspects of
chemical recovery. The mill which is described here has been designed to
allow the production of pulp for either linerboard or sackkraft. When
producing pulp for linerboard, two grades would be made: a high yield I/
(53%) base stock and a normal yield (47%) top stock. For sackkraft only
normal yield pulp, similar to top stock, would be produced. The pulp mill
would have a nominal capacity of 470 air-dry tons per day. This is below the
size of modern large pulp mills which oftern have single-line capacities of
750 to 1,000 tons per dayI, but it is still considered an economically sized
unit. Above 500 tons per day, the marginal benefits from economy of scale
drop off considerably.
1/   Yield: the ratio of screened pulp produced to wood pulped, both on
a bone-dry weight basis.



ANNEX 5
Page 5
Modern mills have a choice of batch or continuous digesters in
which to cook wood by the kraft processes. Within these two major groups,
there are also a number of equipment variations.  The choice of batch or
continuous digesters for modern mills remains a subject of much discussion
and controversy as both methods have advantages and disadvantages specific
to each mill.
The consulting engineer has recommended the use of batch digesters
as being most appropriate for this project. In recent years, about as many
large new pulp mills have been built using batch as continuous digesters.
The choice can be again reviewed at the design development stage.
It is proposed to use six unlined carbon steel digesters, each with
a volume of 135 cubic meters. External cooking liquor circulation and in-
direct liquor heating would be utilized. A complete cooking cycle would
require four hours in each digester. Separate blow tanks would be provided
for high yield and normal yield pulp. The blow lines would be arranged so
that any digester could be blown to either blow tank.
The pulp washing and screening system would consist of two lines,
a large one for base stock and a small one for top stock. Base stock
would pass from the base stock blow tank through a certrifugal precleaner
to remove rocks and tramp metal, and then through double disc refiners to
defiber the partially cooked high-yield pulp. The refined base stock
would then be pumped through two stages of hot stock pressure screens. The
screen rejects (knots and fiber bundles) would be disc refined in separate
equipment and returned to the base stock blow tank. The screened pulp
would flow to a 4 - stage counter-current vacuum washing system. Washed
pulp would be pumped to a high density storage tank.
Top stock pulp would be pumped to a knotter, then on to a 3-stage
countercurrent washing system, followed by a single-stage screening system,
a vacuum thickener and a high density storage tank. Rejects from the
knotters and screens would be sent to the base stock refiners.
When the mill is to produce pulp for sackkraft, the entire produc-
tion, although reduced (283 vs. 477 TPD of paper) would be in normal yield
pulp. The base stock washing and screening system would be mofified to a
system similar to the small top stock line. This would necessitate a shut-
down of this part of the plant during changeover. The paper mill, however,
would have to shut down only while the base stock pulp remaining was Cleaned
out of the system so that there would be no contamination with base stock
during startup or multiwall sack paper.
Two by-products would be recovered in the pulp mill: turpentine
and sulphate soap. Turpentine would be condensed from the vapors relieved
during the early part of each digester cook. Sulphate soap would be
separated by floatation from the residual cooking ("black") liquor during



ANNEX 5
Page 6
the evaporation process prior to chemical recovery. Sulphate soap is often
converted to crude tall oil at the pulp mill, but in this case would be
sold as soap. Tall oil is the principal raw material for a variety of
natural resins used in paint and paper manufacture.
Waste heat from the digester blow system would also be recovered.
The hot water so produced would be used for pulp washing.
The black liquor recovered in the pulp washing operation would
first be concentrated in multiple effect evaporators and forced-circulation
concentrators to a solids level of 65%. This heavy hot black liquor would
be burned in a chemical recovery furnace, from which the molten chemicals
are drawn off the bottom and dissolved in weak wash liquor from the caustic-
izing plant to form green liquor. The gases from the recovery furnace are
passed through a waste heat boiler and economizer, and an electrostatic
precipitator to recover particulate matter, which is recycled.
The green liquor would be recausticized with lime to white liquor
for cooking. Lime sludge from the recausticizing system would be fed to
a rotary kiln to be calcined for re-use. Lime dust would be recovered
from the kiln flue gas by a venturi scrubber and recycled.
The collection and treatment of malodorous gases, other air pollu-
tants and water pollutants from the pulp mill would be practiced extensively
in order to minimize the effect upon the environment (See Annex 8).
Description of Paper Mill
Linerboard or sackkraft would be produced on a single paper machine
and ancilliary equipment, although the machine would be designed primarily
for linerboard manufacture. The machine will be economically sized at 450
TPD - although linerboard machines of up to to 1,400 TPD are in use. Base
stock (80% of the furnish) would be refined in 5 double disk refiners with
800 hp installed on each. Refined base stock would be pumped through
pressure screens and a centrifugal cleaner to the primary headbox of the
sheet former ("fourdrinier"), where it becomes the bottom layer of the
finished linerboard. The top stock (20% of the furnish) would be similarly
but more thoroughly refined in 3 similar double disc refiners, 3 stages of
centrifugal cleaners and through the secondary headbox about half way down
the fourdrinier where it becomes the top layer of the finished linerboard.
The top stock is prepared so that it will provide a better and brighter
printing surface than the coarser, darker base stock could provide for the
corrugated containers to be made from the linerboard.
When producing multiwall sack paper, the paper machine would
utilize elements of both the base stock and top stock systems, but all the
stock would be fed through the primary headbox onto the paper machine.



ANNEX 5
Page 7
The paper machine would have all the proven modern features avail-
able, and would have a finished paper width of 6.4 meters and a maximum de-
sign speed of 600 mpm. It would have two features not found on all liner-
board machines: a breaker stack and a size press. The breaker stack would
densify the linerboard, thereby improving its stiffness and minimizing the
rough surface effects inherent in using a coarse fiber.  The size press
would apply a surface sizing, essentially a prepared starch solution, which
would in effect "glue down" the coarse fibers that would otherwise protrude
from the sheet and interfere with good printing results.
In the most common roll sizes, three rolls of linerboard or six
rolls of multiwall sack paper would be produced across the paper machine.
Waste paper generated during paper making and finishing operations would be
recycled through the base stock system.
As the Antalya region is favored by a warm climate, outdoor-type
construction would be used to achieve economies in structural costs, and
to the extent considered practical, process equipment and storage tanks would
be located outside of buildings. This would apply to the digesters, evapo-
rators, lime kiln, recausticizing equipment, and virtually all pulp and liquor
storage tanks.
Main process buildings would be constructed with reinforced con-
crete floors, reinforced concrete frames, steel roof trusses, and walls of
asbestos cement panels or bricks. Roofs would be pre-cast concrete decks
with built-up asphalt and gravel roofing.
Ground drilling investigations indicate the need for pile found-
ations to support the heavier plant machinery. All other foundations would
consist of spread footings.
As noted elsewhere, all transport (except for fuel oil) would be
by truck during the initial years of operation of the plant. Fuel oil
would be delivered by tankship to an offshore underwater pipeline terminal.
It has been estimated that by 1986 there will be sufficient wood coming
from Adana-Mersin to justify the full time use of a tug and barge. Further
barge traffic could be developed in finished products to Istanbul, Izmir and
other port cities. A barge dock complete with rock breakwater would be
provided at that time.
A townsite would be provided to house key people and other social
infrastructure. Top management would be provided five houses of 150 square
meters each; middle management and other professional staff, 35 houses at
100 square meters; for key operating personnel, 137 houses at 70 square
meters; a 1,000 square meter guest house; and a recreation complex, commissary,
school and mosque.



ANNEX 5
Page 8
Employment
The proposed sawmill and pulp and paper mill would employ some
850 people requiring a wide range of skills as shown in the following
table.
Analysis of Personnel Skills
Pulp and         Sawmill and
Category                             Paper           Wood Handling    Total
Management and Supervisory               21                 15             36
Other Technical/Professional             25                  3            28
Skilled Operators                        72                 27            99
Skilled Tradesmen                       119                41            160
Unskilled and Semi-skilled              317                217           534
Total                                   554               303            857
For the purpose of the table, those personnel who provide general
services to both the pulp and paper mill and to the sawmill have been
included in the pulp and paper mill group.
The Management and Supervisory group would normally all be hired
from existing pulp, paper, and sawmills, or other industries employing
similar and closely related skills. For example, the Pulp Mill Superinten-
dent must have specific experience in the industry either as a Pulp Mill
Superintendent or senior experienced foreman in another mill. The Traffic
Manager, on the other hand, need not have direct pulp mill experience,
providing his experience in traffic management in some other industry is
sufficiently broad.
The other technical and professional group should, to some extent,
be drawn from people with direct pulp and paper or sawmill experience. For
example, included in this group are four engineers, of which at least two
should preferably have direct pulp and paper experience. On the other hand,
the staff doctor and nurse, could obviously be drawn from general practice.
The majority of the skilled operator group will have direct exper-
ience in their jobs elsewhere in the industry.
The skilled tradesmen group requires less actual industry experience,
as in most instances their trade skills are transferable. However, the trades
foremen which are included in the group will have direct industry experience
in order to be able to cope with special maintenance problems encountered
in pulp, paper and sawmills.

ANNEX 5
Page 9
More than 60% of the emploa7ees are classified as partly skilled or
semi-skilled. This group will be drawn from the existing local population.
The nearest urban centre is the town of Manavgat with a population of over
10,000. Antalya city has a population of over 100,000. In_addition to
these two centres there are numerous other small towns and villages within
a 50 km radius, and no trouble is anticipated in hiring from this population
base the estimated 534 people in the unskilled and semi-skilled group.
Recruiting the number (145) of personnel requiring direct industry
experience could prove to be a difficult undertaking. In the existing new
mills of SEKA there is already a shortage of highly experienced personnel
and there appears to be a definite reluctance on the part of Turkish workers
to move to new locations.
In order to overcome these difficulties, SEKA will undertake an
immediate program of up-training in their existing mills to be prepared for
the requirements of an Antalya mill. The training program will be developed
as part of the implementation activities and will be an extension of SEKA's
existing on-the-job training efforts. No difficulties are expected in
getting a satisfactory program launched once the consultants (see below)
are hired and the project is underway.
Other Plant Inputs
The purchase of electric power for the entire project from the
public utility serving the Antalya area was weighed against an on-site power
generation from waste heat and rejected. Although the public utility could
supply the necessary 24 MW at a high, but not unreasonable price, the reli-
ability of the supply because of frequent power failures, was less than the
pulp and paper plant could safely tolerate. Electric power will be purchased
only for those parts of the complex outside of the pulp and paper mill
proper, principally the sawmill and wood preparation plant. This would also
provide a source of electric power for plant startup.
The source of process water for the mill would be the Manavgat
River which provides a flow with a good margin of safety (see Annex 8).
Design water flow for the proposed mill would be 0.65 m3/s. Water quality
requirements for unbleached grades are not particularly demanding.
The water from the Manavgat River will require treatment to remove
suspended solids and iron. The water treatment and supply system would be
sized to handle 60,000 m3/day (0.694 m3/sec). Cooling water from the turbo-
generator condenser would be passed through a cooling tower to minimize
water usage.
Although the pulping chemicals would be recovered, there would be
inevitable losses, which would be made up by the addition of 60 kg/ton
(of paper) of saltcake and 30 kg/ton of limestone. These chemicals are
readily available from natural deposits in Turkey.



ANNEX 5
Page 10
A number of manufactured chemicals, principally papermaking ad-
ditives, would also be required. All these are now made in Turkey except
alum and gum.
About half of the process steam requirements would be provided
by the recovery boiler, and the other half would be generated in a separate
power boiler from heavy fuel oil and bark. The same fuel oil would be
used in the lime kiln and a3 a start-up auxilliary fuel in the recovery furnace.
Fuel oil requirements (without taking bark into account) would reach 77,500
tpa at plant capacity and would be supplied by tankship from the refinery at
Mersin.
Implementation
The industrial part of the project would be implementeod by SEKA
assisted by the Consulting Engineers, Sandwell and Company, Ltd., of
Vancouver, Canada. A contract has been signed between SEKA and Sandwell
to cover design development, detailed design, field supervision of
construction, and operational assistance until commercial operation is
under way.
The proposed implementation scheme is set forth in bar chart form
at the end of this Annex (World Bank 7846).
The first six months would be given over to design development.
During this period, the Consulting Engineer would expand in detail on the
technical and capital cost aspects of the feasibilities studies and would
bring the project to the point where all major technical decisions have
been made, capital costs defined in detail, administrative procedures
established, and all major equipment contracts could be let.  This work
would be carried out in the Consultant's office.  The results of this program
would be presented in a construction budget which would include a description
of the mill, basic arrangement drawings and flow diagrams, detailed capital
cost estimates, and detailed schedules for carrying out the work.
The construction budget would be the basis for control throughout
the project and it is therefore important that concepts agreed upon during
de.i:n development not be changed unless such changes are fully evaluated,
found proritable and approved by the Client for inclusion in the project.
Immediately upon SEKA's approval of the construction budget, which
is scheduled by July 1 , 1974, the Consulting Engineer would provide the
staff and facilities to launch tne detailed design program on a broad
front in their offices. Again, SEKA would have staff in the Consultant's
office who would participate in the work. The purchasing program for major
equipment would be concluded promptly to ensure the flow of engineering
design information from the vendors; control documentation for monitoring



AMIEX 5
Page I1
job progress would be established; site preparation contracts would be let
and secondary equipment purchasing commenced; and the preparation of detailed
structural, civil, mechanical, electrical and instrumentation drawings
started and completed in accordance with the schedule requirements.
Procurement would begin with the issuance of detailed specifications
and requests for tenders for all the major equipment about July 1, 1974.
Procurement would be on a toint basis with EIB under 1BRD rules for inter-
national competitive bidding. After evaluation of the tenders received for
the major equipment, it would be apparent whether the EIB loan could be fully
utilized without resorting to allocating some items to EIB during the later
secondary procurement phase. If not, appropriate allocation to EIB would
then have to be made. Procurement would also involve a final determination
of which equipment made in Turkey would be acceptable on this project.
Project control documentation would include engineering work lists
updated monthly, engineering progress reports, weekly construction progress
reports and construction cost accounting reports; the detailed engineering and
drawing program would be scheduled to dovetail with the planned construction
sequence.
The Consulting Engineer would also provide engineering supervision
at the site throughout the construction period, which would begin with site
preparation work about October 1, 1974. A nucleus field group consisting of
engineers from the Consulting Engineer and SEKA would be at the site to
control the field activities from the commencement of construction. The
nucleus staff would gradually be expanded as construction activity increased,
and the specialists on the staff would change in accordance with need. The
final work of the field engineers would be to assist in the checking out of
machinery and equipment and their interconnections prior to the full start-up,
which is scheduled to occur in the sawmill in early 1976 and in the pulp and
paper mill in early 1978.
It will be noted that the scheduled pulp and paper design and con-
struction period is four years, which is somewhat longer than would normally
be required. Every effort should be made to reduce the time to startup; it
is estimated that some 120 million TL (US$8.55 million) in price escalation
alone could be saved for every 12 months reduction in the schedule.
Operational Assistance Program
Because of the scarcity in Turkey of managerial, technical and
supervisory personnel witn the requisite skills, the Consulting Engineer
would also be engaged to provide operational assistance during the initial
years of project operation.
Preoperational assistance would be given starting in 19774 with a
sawmill manager and training coordinator, and would expand as required into
operations.  It is expected that all expatriates would be phased out by



ANNEX 5
Page 12
October 1, 1978. The purpose of the program would be to assist SEKA with the
preparation of the startup, the commissioning of the facilities and the
training of the mill personnel to satisfactory levels of proficiency in the
shortest possible time.
The general scope of the assistance program would be as follows:
1.  The provision of key expatriate personnel as required to achieve
the objectives of the program.
2. The establishment of an organization comprising the Consultant's
personnel and such other of SEKA's personnel as shall be necessary
for the operation of the mills.
3. The development of a detailed program of work which would be
presented in the form of network diagrams and which would identify
the sequence, timing and inter-relationships of all management
activities necessary for start-up and operation of the mills.
4. The development and supervision of training programs as required
for all personnel associated with the project.
5. The supervision of and assistance with preparation of instruction
manuals for the operation and maintenance of the mills.
6. Supervision of and assistance with the preparation of Policy and
Procedure Manuals where required to define the activities of the
organization.
7. Supervision of and assistance with the preparation of product
manufacturing and raw material specifications.
8.   Supervision of and assistance with the preparation of operating
budgets and standards against which performance after start-up
can be measured.
9. Participation in the acceptance procedures developed by the
engineering Consultant for taking over plant and equipment from
the supply contractors.
Whereas the precise requirements for expatriate personnel depend
upon the availability of trained personnel in Turkey with experience of the
degree required, it is presently envisaged that the expatriate team would
consist of the following:



ANNEX 5
Page 13
-    Manager of Sawmill and Wood Preparation
-    Coordinator, Systems and Training
-    Wood preparation Superintendent
-    Sawmill Shift Supervisor
-    Head Filer
-    Head Sawyer
-    Manager of Pulp and Paper Mill
-    Pulp Mill Superintendent
-    Paper Mill Superintendent
-    Utilities and Chemical Recovery Superintendent
-    Pulp Mill Shift Supervisor
-    Paper Mill Shift Supervisor
-    Engineering and Maintenance Superintendent
Each of the expatriates would be associated with a Turkish counter-
part, who, by the completion of the assistance program would have assumed
fully the responsibilities initially assumed by the expatriate specialist.
Conclusions
The Industrial Project is well prepared and ready for implementa-
tion. The conceptual design is nearly complete, so that detailed design
and engineering can commence now that the Consultant is hired. SEKA are
enthusiastic about working with consultants in the manner above described
because for the first time it will involve them intimately in the details
of the Project implementation. The stage is therefore set to get off with
a good start.

2FJTURE STTDIES
BAt2KGROUND
The series of studies that led up to the definition of the Antalya
Project, particularly the final feasibility study, have been well accepted
in Turkey by the relevant Governnent agencies, particularly the MF/GDP and
SEKA who will use thi's approach for the development of further forest industry
projects to meet the future needs of Turkey for forest products.
Since plans were already well advanced for the development of forest
industries in the northwest part of Turkey, tne government and the interna-
tional agencies 11 in January 1973 agreed that priority should be given to this
area.  An initial study of the Marmara and western Black Sea regions was under-
taken -- defined as a preliminary review at the preinvestment level, of the
size and nature of forest industry which the area might support. It was recog-
nized that some difficulties would be encountered in carrying out such a re-
gional study due to the absence of a national forest development plan.
At the request of the Government, the UNDP agreed to provide funds
for this preliminary study.  The Food and Agriculture Organization was appointed
executing agency for the project and Sandwell and Company Limited were retained
to do the work. The following subsections are based on their report.
Forest Operations
The northwest sector of Turkey along the Sea of Marmara and Western
Black Sea Coast (see "Marmara Region" on MAP 1), amounting to more than six
million hectares, comprises 32 percent of the forested area of Turkey and
contains a standing volume of 416,000,000 m3 overbark of decidous and conifer-
ous species. The present annual allowable cut (AAC) from the region would
satisfy projected demands for industrial logs only until 1977. Several alter-
native approaches to forest development and management were considered in the
study, to increase the production of industrial logs. The annual allowable
cut (AMC) for each approach would be:
1/   UNDP, FAO, IBRD



ANNEX 6
Page 2
Total Volume     Industrial Log
from High Forest     Production
1,000 m3          1,000 m3
Overbark          Underbark
Current MOF AAC                            7,700             4,500
Modified AAC /1                            7,900             4,800
Potential AAC, 1983 /2                     8,700             5,400
Accelerated Cut /3
- 1 million m3/A underbark             10,200             6,400
- 1.5 million m3/A underbark           10,900            6,900
Maximum AAC, 1993 /4                      15,700            11,000
/1   Involves a reduction of rotation age until current annual increment
approximately equals mean annual increment.
/2   Calls for minimum rotation age for pulpwood species and afforestation
with high yield exotic pines with short rotation.
/3   A substantial volume of timber which would become available by 2003
from afforestation with exotic pines thereby allowing an accelerated
cutting of the mature and overmature existing forest.
/4   Involves adopting an afforestation program to plant triple the area
presently planned by the General Directorate of Afforestation (GDA);
the indicated AAC is achievable by 1993.
While the alternative AAC's examined are technically feasible, a significant
increase in forest operation would be required. For example, if the accel-
erated cutting of 1.5 million m3/A is adopted, the overall annual planting
program including reforestation would have to increase from 22,000 hectares
in 1973 to a possible 78,000 hectares in 1983. The major problems of finan-
cing, organization, and logistics associated with massive afforestation pro-
grams have been successfully resolved in other countries. One of the most
successful examples is in Brazil where the annual rate of afforestation has
been increased from 38,000 hectares in 1967 to 293,000 hectares in 1972. In
the Mediterranean area, Morocco, Portugal, and Spain have successful afforesta-
tion programs.

(a)  The sulphite mill will be shut down at Izmit to reduce pollution,

outside the study area, in which substantially all current

(e)  About 110,000 tons of particleboard be developed within the

of the national demand.

Costs, Profits
Rough estimates of the costs -- both capital and operating -- for
the various schemes, the gross income and hence, the gross profit, were made.
The Capital cost of forestry operations would be some 2.5 billion
TL ($180 million) between 1973 and 1982 and the gross return on this invest-
ment would be about 20 percent.
The capital cost of the various industrial alternatives range from
6.1 (Case IC) to 7.4 (Case II) billion TL ($435 to 530 million) with the gross
returns varying between 18 and 20%.
Conclusions
Two main conclusions emerged from the study:
(a) Viable forest industry developments can be foreseen for the
Marmara area which could satisfy Turkey's forest product needs
to the early 1980's.
(b) However, they would require a major change in forest management
(basically massive afforestation and accelerated cutting of
existing stands) and do not adequately take into account de-
velopments in other forest areas in Turkey.
FUTURE STUDIES PROGRAM
From the Marmara Pilot Study the outline of the program of future
studies that will be necessary to ensure timely development of Turkey's
forest resources emerges. The program would encompass several types of
studies:
--   Rough assessments of forest resources in other parts
of Turkey along the lines of the Marmara Pilot Study.
-- National policy papers on such issues as level of
environmental protection, system of forest management,
private versus public development, the place of the
cottage saw-mill, assessment of national demand for
forest products, etc.
-- Detailed techr.o-economic feasibility studies for spe-
cifically defined projects such as that done for the
Antalya Project.



ANNEX 6
Page 8
Given the level of likely investment - $600 -- $700 million in
only the Marmara region -- it is possible to roughly estimate the cost of
the studies by comparison to the Antalya case. On the Antalya Project the
feasibility study cost some $400,000 in foreign costs and 2.5 million TL in
local costs, a total of $580,000 equivalent and the investments will total
about $165 million equivalent. Thus for each $1 million of investment about
$3,500 of study cost were needed $2,400 of which was for foreign consultants.
If the same ratio applies, the feasibility studies needed for the Marmara
region alone would cost about $2.3 million.
The UINDP and FAO have made tentative estimates of study costs by
a preliminary identification of individual studies and an assessment of these
costs. The tentative estimates amount to $2.5 - 3.5 million.
FINANCING A STUDY PROGRAM
The UNDP and the EIB have both indicated willingness to participate
in financing the foreign exchange portion of the studies. They have urged
IBRD to also participate as the Bank is likely to be called upon to finance
continued investments and would be better placed to do so if it were intimately
involved in the study program.
A tentative financing scheme has been proposed in which,
-    the UNDP would contribute about $1 million, partly from
the Turkey Country Program and partly fromn the Adminis-
trator's Program Reserve, specifically the Investment
Feasibility Studies Fund;
-    the EIB would contribute $1.1 million from its existing
line of credit of 2.0 u.a. (about $2.5 million) to Turkey
for preinvestment studies, and
-    IBRD would contribute $0.4 as a "piggy-back" operation
to the Antalya Project.
The total funds of $2.5 million would be supplemented by Government
funds for local activities and possibly also for foreign expenses.
IMPLEMENTATION OF THE PROGRAM
Much remains to be done to be done to finalize the details of im-
plementing the program, even though agreement in principle among all the par-
ticipating agencies of the need for and urgency of the program has been
reached.
The first step will be a joint meeting of the UNDP, FAO, EIB, IBRD
and relevant Government agencies to draw up a detailed list of studies, their
te-,ms of reference, budget, timing, and method of execution. Assignment of



ANNEX 6
Page 9
specific studies to specific financing sources will follow. A means of coor-
dinating the future activities, particularly in Turkey, will have to be de-
vised. Certainly regular progress meetings will be held at which the inter-
ested parties will participate.
The joint meeting is presently being planned and should be held in
early January 1974. The first studies could be commissioned by March 1974 with
completion by the end of that year. Major investments could thus be called
for by early 1975.
IBRD PARTICIPATION
In view of
-    the importance of this national resource based sector
of Turkey's economy;
-    the significant role IBRD has played thus far in its
development;
-    the need for massive funding for its future development,
and
-    the likelihood that IBRD will be called upon to help in
the financing.
It is recommended that IBRD funds of up to $0.4 million be made
available to the Government for studies, to be spent at the discretion of
Bank Management in accordance with normal Bank practice for preinvestment
studies.

TRANSPORTATION
Transportation for the project's paper products will be arranged
and paid for by the purchasers. Tne detailed marketing and distribution sys-
tem for the sawnwood has not been finalized but will be studied and imple-
mented as part of the Project. In the Antalya region large quantities of raw
materials as well as fuel oil and other supplies will have to be transported
by SEKA.
The average annual quantities of the major items to be transported
are:
586,000 m           Wood from Antalya region to Mill,
average distance 125 km
112,000 m3          Wood from Adana/Mersin to Mill,
average distance 400 km

400 km

estimated average distance 600 km

Existing Facilities
The Antalya region is almost entirely dependent on truck
transportation. No rail facilities exist. The nearest rail connections are
at Isparta 150 kilometers to the north, and at Mersin 450 kilometers to the
east. Sea transportation is limited to small coastal vessels generally hand-
ling bulk items such as coal. Their size, configuration, and absence of
handling gear do not favor their use for paper products. Within Turkey, road
systems are well developed and generally of a high standard. Within Antalya,
the coast road from Antalya to Adana-Mersin is narrow and winding. The road
from Antalya to the Anatolean Plateau is winding with many steep grades.  Thus
within the Antalya region there are some speed and vehicle size restrictions.
The privately owned trucking system has a sufficient amount of trucks
to provide necessary transportation for the wood, other inputs and finished
products of the Project. Truck availability is said to exceed demand by a
wide margin. The truck industry is reported to be operating nation-wide at



ANNEX 7
Page 2
only 40% capacity. Most trucks it Turkey are 5 - 8 ton, single-axle units
usually owner-driven. Larger trucks cannot be contemplated for the Project
unless highway improvements are carried out by the Turkish Government on the
Antalya-Isparta road.
Wood Hauling
When full capacity is reached in 1981, it is estimated that wood
transport will require one truck to arrive every three minutes at tne plant
site during working hours. The large number of trucks will require:
- Very careful planning between SEKA and MOF that the right truck
is sent to the right part of the forest at the right time to
pick up the right load i.e., logistics planning will become a
major task for the Mill Management.
- Improvement of the existing highway near the mill, particularly
the section through the town of Manavgat (a bypass may be
needed), the section from Manavgat to the mill site, and access
from the highway to the mill road (over- or underpass).
In addition, it is likely that constraints on truck availability
could arise at certain seasons during the peak hauling of agricultural
produce.
Product Hauling
Typical rates from the Antalya millsite to the key market centres
and for different truck types have been calculated and presented below:

At present, there is a wide range of rates for truck freight depending upon
the availability of backhaul cargo, seasonal demand, road conditions and com-

Potential for Sea Transportation
In view of the large quantities and distances in truck transporta-
tion involved, other possibilities such as sea transport have been studied by
Sandwell.



A-NNEX 7
Page 3
It is believed that the coast of Turkey offers great potential for
the development of a sea transportation system employing tugs and barges.
In terms of Antalya such a system would be attractive because:
1.  Three of the four major consumption centres are on the coast.
2.  Distances are well within the normally accepted economic
working range of a tug and barge system.
3.  The small tidal differences minimize loading and unloading
difficulties.
4.  The incidence of storms is low and there are many areas of
protected water which offer shelter.
The cost of transportation of all product shipments from Antalya
to water destinations has been developed using a tug and barge system. The
estimate is based on an annual quantity of 120,000 tons of paper and 75,000
tons of sawnwood. No backhaul cargo has been included. The estimate is
based on the use of 3,000 ton barges and 1,500 hp tugs. Where possible tan-
dem tows using two barges have been used.
Based on the foregoing, two tugs and seven barges would be required
at a total investment of 50,000,000 TL. Construction of the vessels is with-
in the capability of the Turkish shipbuilding industry although some compo-
nents, mainly engines, would need to be imported.
Some improvement in dock facilities might be required at the receiv-
ing ports but this has not been included in the estimates.
Freight rates using a tug and barge system have been estimated and
compared with truck rates in the table below. Present and potential freight
rates for product shipments from Antalya to main market centres include all
operating and capital charges.
Comparative Freight Rates
Rate TL/ton
Destination          Tug and Barge        6 Ton Truck    20 Ton Truck
Istanbul                 171                  270              214
Izmir                    146                  224              170
Mersin                   158                  145         Not UsabLe
Conclusion
The foregoing estimates clearly indicate that savings in product
freight costs can be achieved by use of either large trucks or a tug and barge
system. It is conceivable that if sufficient coast traffic from other indus-
tries could be developed, particularly of a backhaul nature, that further



ANNEX 7
Page 4
reductions in the tug and barge freight rates over and above those indicated
might be achieved.
There are however certain pertinent observations which must be
made:
(a) Under the present Turkish market system, the mill will not
have to sell its paper products on a delivered basis nor
is it in any way required to arrange transportation.
Therefore, no savings would accrue to the project itself
from implementation of a tug and barge system on over 60
percent of the cargo shipped because the customer pays
whatever freight rate is involved.
(b) The existing privately owned trucking system has sufficient
trucks to provide necessary delivery service.
Based on the foregoing, there would be little justification for the
Antalya Establishment itself to become involved in the investment and opera-
tions of sea transport facilities. However, as the data reveals, considerable
savings and benefits in economic terms would accrue, particularly if the
potentials for other industries are taken into consideration. Therefore, it
is recommended that the Government seriously consider a study evaluating the
various aspects of the establishment of sea transport facilities. Studies of
road improvements around Manavgat should also be made.
With regard to the wood raw material and other supplies, the
Antalya Establishment cannot avoid deep involvement in the transport economics
and arrangements. As part of the project implementation (see Annex 5), SEKA
and its consultants will, at an early stage, have to prepare detailed trans-
portation plans. In particular, they will have to establish the basis on
which they will engage the private truckers to ensure that they meet their
commitments. The costs of these planning activities are included under the
"consultant services" in the capital cost estimate.



ANNEX 8
Page 1
PLANT SITE AND ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT
Background
The Antalya area is well known for natural beauty, historical sites,
and delightful climate.  Consequently, it stands high on the priority list
for development by the Ministry of Tourism. Pulp and paper plants are equally
well known for their degrading effect on the environment where they are lo-
cated.  Simultaneous development in the Antalya region of tourism and a large
pulp and paper plant would therefore seem to be on a collision course.
However, from the start of studies to develop the area (especially
the Poyry and Sandwell studies), it was recognized that modern tecnology
would enable reduction of the degrading effect of a pulp and paper plant to a
level where it would not interfere with tourism or the population. Detailed
examination of plant sites away from the major tourist areas were made even
though cost penalties would result. An interministerial committee composed
of representatives of all interested Government ministries, including Tourism,
the SP0, and SEKA, evaluated 10 potential plant sites in the Manavgat area,
and finally selected the site furthest from Side, the major tourist develop-
ment in the area. SEKA agreed to this selection even though the site will
cost over TL 45 million more than the site SEKA preferred.
Great care has gone into minimizing harmful effects the plant may
have by:
-    detailed evaluation of 10 plant sites;
-    in-plant design to maximize recycling of process water and
air and thereby minimize harmful effluents being released
to the environment;
-    inclusion of effluent treatment facilities;
-    ensuring that the plant site will have the maximum
aesthetic appeal.
Choice of Plant Site
The selection of a pulp mill site is almost always a compromise
since requirements are often contradictory. Ideally, one would want:
a location which minimizes transportation cost - near
the centre of wood supply;



ANNEX 8
Page 2
good connections to take products to major business
centres;
an adequate and economical supply of process water;
an economic means of disposing of liquid effluents and
where the effects of air emissions will be minimal;
an economical supply of fuel and electric power;
good physical conditions such as sufficient land available
(also for expansion), good foundation conditions, no need
for major earthworks, and protection from floods and
earthquakes;
-    a location close to population and recreation centres to
provide a source of operating labor, housing, and the
required social facilities such as schools, hospitals and
stores.
The prime choice, dictated by the topography (mountains of the
Anatolian Plateau, and a coastal plain along the Mediterranean Sea) was a main
highway going from Antalya to Alanya.  Only in two places do the spur roads
of the coastal highways penetrate to the interior - one is the main link to
Ankara via Isparta, and tlhe other a much more inferior road to Konya.
Wood must go first to the coastal road before going anywhere else.
An inland location, studies show, would have enormous transportation costs.
There are also difficulties of water supply and effluent disposal.
Transportation Situation
If the maximum amount of wood available to the project is all
delivered to any one of the potential sites, the average wood cost is vir-
tually equal as shown by transport studies.  The reason for this is the
spreadout nature of the forest resources over the whole region (see also
Map 1). Regarding transport connections for product shipment, the sites all
require the building of a high quality road to connect with the main highway.
The length of this connecting road does not exceed 10 km for any site, and is
only 2 km for the selected site.
Water Supply.
The availability of process water to supply the plant's needs of
nearly 1 m3/sec is difficult and uncertain at all potential sites except
those with access to Manavgat River. Only the Manavgat River has sufficient
excess water when present and future irrigation potential is taken into account,
as shown in the following Table.

Page 3

Disposal of Effluents
At all the sites, treated liquid effluents would be discharged into
the sea. The effluent would be treated to render it harmless to the receiv-
ing water and to reduce its coloring effect so it will not be noticed. This
will necessitate construction of on-shore treatment facilities and a sub-
marine disposal pipeline long enough that the effluent will be discharged at
a depth and in an area of current activities to ensure it is adequately
diluted. No serious physical difficulties are expected in laying the
approximately 1.5 km underwater portion of the pipeline.
The current thinking on disposal of gaseous emissions is that after
in-plant treatment to reduce odour and noxious gases to the minimum, they
would be gathered for disposal to the atmosphere through a single high stack.
This should give sufficient lift and dispersion to any remaining pollutants
to make them undetectable at ground level except during periods of major
plant malfunction - which should be rare.
No serious air pollution problem is therefore expected and a1l
the sites could be described as acceptable in this respect. However, the
sites do differ in the proximity of settled and/or future population, and
prevailing wind directions and therefore in the marginal effect that gaseous
emissions may have. The prevailing wind direction is on- and off- shore
breezes, and only 2% of the time with east and west winds that blow along
the beaches.
Aesthetic Effect
Factory buildings and plant layout will be designed for high
aesthetic standards as well as technical performance. The use of landscaping,
natural and planted screenings of trees, and screenings from the lay of land
will all be used to greatest effect to prevent the intrusion of the plant
from degrading the natural beauty of the area.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
Fuel and Power Supplie
Electrical power is available at all sites from the private company
which operates the Kepez Power Station and which buys power from the state-
operated Turkish Electrical Authority grid (TEK). Some differences in trans-
mission line costs will occur between sites. The average power cost would be
appr. 200 TL/14iH, about twice the price paid in North America.
However, most of the power needs will be generated on site (93%)
using the steam generated in the chemical recovery and power boilers. Power
supply from the grid is not reliable enough to be the major source of energy.
Supplemental fuel needs will be met by heavy fuel oil delivered by tankships
from the refinery at Mersin to an offshore submarine pipeline unloading sta-
tion.
Physical Site Conditions
The chosen site has sufficient land for an industrial complex of
the size contemplated. Future expansion would not be hampered by lack of
space.
Environmental Impact
SEKA is already engaged in carefully assessing the impact of the
plant on the environment and ensuring that adequate measures are taken to
keep the impact within tolerable limits. SEKA therefore hired consultants
from TW Beak and Co., a subsidiary of Sandwell and Company, well reputed
in environmental work, to assist in this effort. The consultants drew up
a matrix to measure the environmental impact - a technique recommended by
the US Geological Survey to put some numbers to otherwise qualitative judge-
ments. A copy of the matrix is given on page 6.
It shows each possible action caused by the construction and
operation of the plant and its impact - positive and negative - with each
segment of the environment. From a scale of 1 to 10, weights are attached
to the magnitude and importance on each segment of the environment.
The matrix shows that the specific actions with the most important
impac-. are:
1.  Pulp Mill Processing
2.   Air Emissions
3.   Aquaeous Emissions
4.   Paper Hill Processing
5.   Wood harvesting



ANNEX 8
Page 5
The environmental characteristics most affected by the proposed
actions are:
1.   Scenic View (Aesthetics)
2.   Ocean Quality at Side
3.   Ocean Quality at Misirilhir (West of $ide)
4.   Ocean Quality at the plant
5.   Bathing
From this assessment and a knowledge of the processes involved,
Beak was able to suggest appropriate remedial measures as shown below:
Remedial Measures to Reduce Impacts
Air Emissions
The characteristic kraft mill odor is due to the presence of a
mixture of hydrogen sulphide, methyl mereaptan, dimethyl sulphide and
dimethyl disulphide.  The characteristic odour threshold values of these
compounds and sulphur dioxide produced in the furna¢es are:

EMISSION         CHARACTERISTIC ODOUR       O1DUR TKIRESHOLD
Hydrogen sulfide          rotten eggs               0.0047 ppm
Methyl mercaptan          rotten cabbage            0.0021 ppm

Sulfur dioxide            acrid, burning            0.47   ppm
In addition, the kraft mill may discharge particulates into the atmosphere
from the recovery furnace, lime kiln and smelt tank (caused mainly by the
carry-over of solids plus the sublimation and condensation of inorganic
chemicals such as sodium sulphate and sodium carbonate).
To reduce odours to the minimum, the pulp mill will be designed
with a low-odour type recovery furnace. Foul condensates will be stripped
and blown to the power boiler where they will be burned and non-condensible
odorous gases from the batch digesters will be collected in a "vaposphere"
and fed to the lime kiln for burning after being first scrubbed with white
liquor to remove sulphur compounds. In addition, black liquor storage tanks
and washer filtrate tanks will be connected to the brown stock washer hood
exhaust and these gases will be burned in the recovery boiler. These inplant
air emission control systems will keep obnoxious air emissions to levels as
low as best available technology offers.
To reduce particulates, an electrostatic precipitator on the re-
covery furnace will remove 99% of particulate emissions. In addition, every
consideration would be given to optimum operation of chip handling and blow-
ing operations to ensure that chip dust does not blow off the property.
However, regardless of how well the mill is designed, there still
will remain some odorous gases which will be discharged into the atmosphere.
The detection of the gases at ground level will be dependent on local meteoro-
logical conditions and the conditions of discharge. Beak concluded that a
stack 130 to 200 m high carrying all discharges, smelt tank, lime kiln, mud
washer, vents flue gases, etc. would probably reduce the ground level odours
to levels below detection. The exact height of the stack would need to be
determined during the detailed engineering design phase of the project. In
addition, a high stack with reheating of the flue gases by combining hotter
drier flue gases from other sources (power boiler) would minimize water vapor
emissions which create fog and which usually invite adverse public reaction
since anything visible coming out of a stack is "pollution". A tall stack
also has the advantage that it does not rely on operating skill for perform-
ance (but the disadvantage of being clearly visible).
It is therefore concluded that the pulp mill can be designed and
properly operated to virtually eliminate any noticeable impact of odour to
local inhabitants or to tourists passing through the area.



A:EX 8
Aquaeous Emissions
Untreated kraft mill wastes give rise to many forms.of water pollu-
tion. They can be conveniently discussed under the following headings:
-    Suspended solids
-    BOD
-    Colour
-    Odor and Taste
Suspended solids include bark, fibre, lime and fly ash which will
settle to the bottom of receiving waters and suffocate bottom dwelling
organisms (invertebrates). Suspended solids also produce aesthetic impairment,
causing fouling of fishing lines and nets, and spoiling of lake or sea bottom
areas for swimming. Some suspended solids float.as fibre mats. However,
most organic suspended solids settle and can form sludge deposits which fre-
quently become anaerobic producing odorous gases.
Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD) concentrations of untreated kraft
mill effluents are high and when discharged to a receiving stream can pro-
duce conditions of reduced oxygen concentration. These low oxygen levels
in natural waters have.a debilitating or damaging effect on the aquatic com-
munity and can cause fish kills. This condition is most severe when.effluents
are discharged to small rivers and lakes where dilution is not great. Effluent
discharges to the sea receive a la:ger dilution and it is not believed that
reduced oxygen levels will create a problem at the proposed mill site.
Kraft mill effluent is often a dark colour which reduces light
penetration through the surface waters thereby inhibiting phytoplankton
productivity. At a dilution of 100;1 after discharge to the sea the colour
of the effluent would be reduced from a conservatively estimated 400 colour
units to 4 colour units. The resultant colour is considered acceptable
under normal conditions. It would not be noticeable in a jar of water, but
most probably.would be observed by boaters and from the air, and would appear
as a yellowish-brown tinge to swimmers in the immediate vicinity.
The wastes have an unpleasant odour and taste, and can cause fish
flesh tainting.
The design of the mill will ensure that effluents contain a mini-
mum of these harmful pollutants when they are discharged for treatment:
-    The basic design philosophy recommends the maximum reuse
of contamiinated process waste waters in the mill. Clean
uncontaminated cooling waters will be separated in a closed
sewer system and discharged to sea by-passing the treat-
ment facilities;



ANNEX 8
Page 9
-    Inpiant measures will include the maximum reduction of
major sources of BOD, odour and toxicity before discharge
to treatment. For example, foul condensates will be
stripped prior to discharge to treatment;
-    the process systems will be designed for operating simpli-
city to minimize upsets or spills.
The two principal areas of concern for liquid waste discharges
will therefore be suspended solids and colour, and the effluent treatment
facilities will be installed to reduce these pollutants. The degree
of treatment will be such as to ensure that under severe conditions, the
effluent is not noticeable to boaters and bathers in the Side region. The
following measures will be taken:
-    maximum suspended solids removal will be achieved using
a primary clarifier with a very low upflow rate of approx-
imately 500 gallons per sq. ft. per day and with an inner
reaction zone where coagulants such as alum and polyelec-
tolyte can be added to enhance suspended solids removal
efficiency. A significant quantity of colour will also be
removed in this process.
-    The sludge from the clarifier will be dewatered and land
disposed on the mill site. Appropriate reclamation of the
disposal site by the planting of trees and grasses will
minimize its aesthetic impact.  The disposal site could
be used for other mill refuse as well.
-    Clarifier supernatent liquid would then flow by gravity
to one of two - 10 hour settling lagoons. A 24 hour em-
ergency spill basin would also be available to collect
both high suspended and dissolved solids spills.
-    The effluent will then be discharged to sea through an
extended diffused outfall long enough to discharge
effluent at a point with sufficient dilution to create a
submerged effluent dispersion field and eliminate any
surface effluent plume. The length of the outfall pipe
would be established following detailed in situ oceano-
graphic monitoring of currents, and temperature-salinity
profiles and could be 1.5 km or more long.
Biological treatment to reduce BOD is not considered necessary be-
cause of the large assimilative capacity of the sea. In the unlikely event
that sea currents are found to be negligible then biological treatment will
have to be considered. Beak also recommends that land irrigation be invest-
igated.



ANNEX 8
Page 10
Aesthetics
The flow of trucks is expected to be about one every three minutes
during working hours. To minimize the cultural impact of this activity, it
is recommended that highway No. 6 between Manavgat and the millsite be improved
to provide good traffic circulation past the site and that rapid exit and
access ramps be built at the jluetion of highway No. 6 and the access road
leading to the lmillsite.   Ocean transport to and from the site would reduce
the number and frequency of trucks to the site substantially and in the long-
term would be considered preferable and more economic (see Annex 7).
The visual impact of industrial buildings can be minimized by
careful architectural design.  The high stack could be painted a colour such
as olive brown to blend with the nattural pastoral setting. Careful location
of social and administrative buildings will enhance the aesthetics as will
attention to'landscaping,'especially the preservation of as many of the
existing-scrub pine trees on the site as possible.
Outline of Follow-Up Studies
The optimum design for the treatment and disposal of air and water
emissions requires that further studies be commissioned immediately. These
would also help to establish a comprehensive pre-operational environmental
baseline. 'Subsequent follow-up post-operational surveys to be performed
annually would then be able to demonstrate what changes, if any, have occurred,
and what corrective' actions may be necessary.
An outline of the studies recommended by Beak is given here:
Air Survey
A small-meteorological station should be installed at the site
to supplement the Government Station at Manavgat to record the following
parameters three times daily:
Wind direction
Wind speed
Ambient temperature and humidity
Precipitation
Cloud coverage
Barometric pressure
In addition, a weather balloon should be available to record the altitude
temperature profile to determine the existence as well as the extent of
inversion conditions.
Approximately five fixed S02 and dustfall stanchion stations
should be constructed in the vicinity of the proposed site. Samples would
be collected monthly at each station to measure dustfall and sulphate rate
prior to startup of the mill, and total reduced sulphur compounds after
startup.



ATEX 8
Page 11
Oceanographic Survey
The direction and magnitude of the subsurface and surface currents
should be determined at different periods of the year. Drift drogue studies
should be conducted during three periods of the year - August, December and
May. In addition, a recording flowmeter should be installed 1.5 - 2.0 km
offshore to provide a continuous record of currents.
Accurate sonar soundings are required in the vicinity of the proposed
outfall extending 4 km from the shoreline. Underwater marine photographs
should be taken along the proposed pipeline route and several core samples
collected from the bottom to point out any problems which might be encountered
in laying the outfall pipe.
Temperature salinity gradients should be established at several
stations to assist in understanding and predicting water movement patterns,
and in calculating effluent buoyancy. Basic seawater characteristics should
be established at the same time. Analyses should include BOD5, dissolved
oxygen, total and dissolved solids, suspended solids, turbidity, pH, colour
and transparency. The chemical characterization studies should be conducted
during offshore wind conditions three times during the year to coincide with
the three proposed current surveys.
A mobile laboratory could be purchased by SEKA for all scientific
anaylses and used later by the mill Environmental Control Department following
plant startup.
Biological Surveys
Four baseline surveys should be conducted over a two year period
prior to startup to collect information on the type of species, number,
average size and other biological data on fish, benthos (macro-invertebrates),
plankton, intertidal organisms and bottom sediments in the waters in the
vicinity of the plant and the proposed outfall discharge. The surveys should
be continued after startup to obtain comparative data to measure the effect
of the plant.
Environmental Impact of Forestry Operations
The principles of forest management will give utmost importance to
maintaining and improving environmental quality concomitant with maximum
yield and optimizing productivity. This will require an intensity of forest
management hitherto not practiced in the Antalya region, and arrangements are
in hand to ensure that it will be available.
Economically, the most desirable method of forest management would
be to concentrate effort into a few large clearcut blocks, but there are
several reasons as to why this is not possible. The available labour force



ANNEX 8
Page 12
could not be fully utilized without wholesale movement of forest villages
and the use of large camps. The requirements of amenity and aesthetics for
the local people and tourists will demand that cutting blocks be limited in
size.
The silvicultural requirements of some species dictate other than
clearcut logging. For example, Abies, a shade demander, must be managed under
a selection system. Where natural regeneration is to be induced, a system of
group cutting or strip clearcutting is indicated. In addition, much of the
forest is made up of scattered small stands, so that the area of forestry
operatons will be controlled and widely distributed. Rather than attempt to
achieve economies of scale with each cutting block it is more appropriate to
concentrate a series of smaller working units within an area and achieve
economies in this manner.
The sizes of clearcut blocks may vary but would not be greater
than 100 ha. Most blocks should be within the 40 - 60 ha range. These size
blocks should not cause serious soil erosion, as soil engineering will be
practiced. Even so, in the event of unusually large amounts of precipitation
any of the feasible erosion control measures may fail. However, it is equally
true that the forest in its present state also fails to prevent disastrous
erosion in such rare instances.
Conclusion
Matters are well in hand to ensure that the industrial plant's impact
on the environment is held within acceptable limits. The implementing
authorities are sensitive to the need to pay strict attention to this matter
and the necessary steps are under way to ensure that the Plant and its
effluent treatment facilities are properly designed, engineered and constru-
cted. In addition, a program has been established to constantly monitor
environmental impacts to ensure that they will not get out of hand.

will have to be built in the forthcoming years (see Annex 16). It is esti-

establish paper production facilities to satisfy the projected growth of
demand and therefore would eliminate a major part of the needs for importa-
tion for the foreseeable future.

local production. Recently there have been reported imports of pulpwood
from Canada, but this is expected to be temporary due to low felling activi-
ties last winter and present unexpected high demand for wood.  In past years,
pulp wood has been imported from Russia. Turkey was also importing sawnwood
before 1963.
Exports of sawnwood are also undertaken but in limited amounts
and mainly in beech and fir from the Black Sea region. In 1972, about 50,000
m3 have been exported. There are no imports of sawnwood.

Forecasted Situation
There have been a number of studies by the FAO/UNDP Project and
by Turkish Government authorities with a view to estimating future sawnwood
requirements. These studies have been based on both regression analysis and
end-use analysis. Regression aaalyses were based on projections of cement
consumption (incorporating the trend of building activities), or Gross
National Product as the independent variable. There was a fairly wide varia-
tion in the projected values in 1977 and 1982 by the application of the three
conventional regression formulae y = a + bx, log y - a + b log x and y - a +
b log x. The comparative values are shown below.

The studies carried out by the FAO Team were more searching in analysis of
wood use in the various end-use applications and, in addition to considering
perpetuation of current building practices, gave consideration to changing
patterns of use likely to be implemented with the adoption of new technology
and materials. The projected demand rates for construction sawnwood in
Turkey under the assumed conditions were:
1.   Continuance of present practices        4.3% annual increase to 1982.
2.   Implementation of new technology
and materials                     3.3% annual increase to 1982.
The conclusion of the FAO/UNDP Project was that the rate of 4.3 percent would
more likely apply since a pronounced trend to new methodology, materials, or
smaller housing units was unlikely to develop in the short-term future.
Since a relatively low growth rate of 4.3% for sawuwood on a
national basis is expected, future roundwood production should be sufficient
to meet the sawnwood demand. But future roundwood needs for paper production
are more difficult to predict since several major paper projects will be
needed within a relatively short period. Therefore it is important that
future paper production is closely tied to wood production as it has been
in the Antalya Project.

Over the twelve year period from 1960 to 1972, the total,indicated
increase in consumption of containerboard has been about 475 percent, at an
average annual rate of 15-1/2 percent, starting from a very low level. Refer-
ence to the year to year growth shows an erratic pattern around the 15-1/2%
average. During the last 10 years, the large state-owned monopoly, Tekel,
which produces and distributes tobacco, alcoholic beverages, beer, sugar and
tea, moved from wooden packing cases to corrugated containers. The impact of
this conversion was very significant since Tekel is presently the largest
user of containers in Turkey. They used about 15 percent of all supplies.
From this time on Tekel's requirements will reflect only the growth in the
demand for its products, since the conversion to corrugated containers is
essentially complete.



ANNEX 9
Page 7
At the time of the field interviews with end-users and converters
there was universal evidence of a major supply deficiency in the market for
containerboard. There is no scientific basis for estimating the degree of
that shortage, since convertors did not keep accurate records of orders which
they refused and undoubtedly much of the indicated shortage in supply repre-
sents duplicated inquiries to two or more converters which would naturally
indicate a stronger market demand than in fact exists.
Estimates of true demand if adequate supplies were available
to converters must be largely subjective. Bearing in mind that some of
those interviewed in 1972 had also been interviewed in 1971 when the market
was in a state of real depression and saw nothing but gloom in the future,
the current demand for containers is probably 20% to 40% above the indicated
consumption for 1972. The Table below is based upon the actual estimated

Consumption in Relation to Other Countries
Among countries using linerboard Turkey ranks low despite the
very rapid relative increases in usage which have occured during the past
decade. It is interesting to see how Turkey stands in relation to two other
countries which are both contiguous in location and may be assumed to have
reached an intermediate position in terms of their economic development.
These two countries are Israel, for which market studies were carried out
as a segment of this report, and Greece.
The Tables below and on the next page compare the usage of
linerboard in Turkey with that in Greece and Israel in relation to some
of these indicators.
