Turkey lags in the usage of both linerboard and packaging. Greece
and Israel use imported supplies of both at internationally competitive prices.
Israel's dominant position is due in large measure to an export-oriented econ-
o;ay.
Sources of Supply
At the time of the market investigations all linerboard being
used in Turkey was obtained from domestic sources. With availability of do-
mestic linerboard from the recently completed paper manufacturing facilities
at Caycuma, regulations have been invoked with a view to curtailing imports.
Statistical reporting procedures are not mandatory in Turkey. The Brussels
nomenclature has been adopted by the Turkish Government in classifying paper
and paperboard grades. Export statistics of the major linerboard exporting
countries in North America and Northern Europe indicate that Turkish imports
were between 5,000 and 8,000 tons in 1970. SEKA operates kraft mills at
Caycuma and Dalaman which produce kraft linerboard. Linerboard production
in Turkey is spasmodic. Linerboard has been manufactured at Izmit, Caycuma
and Dalaman. The market pressure for sackkraft has given that grade priority
at Caycuma. Some linerboard production has been temporarily switched to
the Dalaman mill.



ANNEX 9
Page 9
In addition, to the supplies of linerboard produced by SEKA at
its three mills, some "bogus" linerboard and corrugating medium has been
produced by small mills operating in the Istanbul area. The production from
these mills is small. Their main product line is either boxboard or low
grade wrapping and bag papers. None of them provides a substantial base for
expansion into bogus liner production on a greatly increased scale.
The Corrugated Container Converting Industry
The corrugated container industry in Turkey is new. In the
last 5 years, growth in production has been rapid. It is estimated that in
1960 only about 7,500 tons of containerboard were produced in Turkey (at
Izmit). Current production of containerboard is at the rate of nearly 40,000
tons per year. The following Table lists the corrugated container converting
plants presently operating in Turkey.

Only the three largest of the mills can be regarded as modern converting
operations. The smaller mills employ unsophisticated but often ingenious
home-made equipment which nonetheless turns out containers of acceptable
standards. Three additional plants could be built by 1980. One of these
would be in Izmir, one in Ankara, with a third on the Mediterranean coast.
The Mersin-Adana area appears to be the most logical Mediterranean site in
view of the large potential offered by the fruit industry and the rapid
development of secondary industry in the region,
Despite supply shortages, some of the converters are sales-oriented
and provide a high standard of service.
Use of -Linerboard
The structure of the Turkish economy, the nature of its exports,
and the high cost of corrugated containers when compared to wood, have
combined to restrict the market. Corrugated containers have made headway in
applications where the cost is a subordinate consideration and convenience of
paperboard over wooden cases provides very evident advantages to producers.



ANNEX 9
Page 10
A comparison of the end-use consumption patterns for Turkey and
the United States shows the same general ranking of end-use applications in
both countries with the soft consumer items being the most important product
group, followed by durable consumer products, and industrial products.
In Turkey there is concentration of use in the soft consumer group
of products. This accounts for at least 2/3rd of the total consumption of cor-
rugated containers. Export shipment use is of comparative insignificance. A
further insignificant tonnage is used for shipping of fruit and vegetables.
Likely future trends of this situation are summarized below:
(a)  Wooden cases will continue to be the dominant packaging material;
Corrugated containers will take an increasing share of the market,
especially in the packaging of industrially produced..and higher
value consumer products;
(b) No important conversion from wooden cases in the domestic agri-
cultural trade can be expected until corrugated container prices
are substantially lower than wood;
(c) The export packaging of fruits and vegetables offers the largest
single potential market for corrugated containers, but prices
are still too high. Also, the generally lower quality of the
containerboard, and the absence of integrated systems of trans-
port, storing and merchandizing contribute to this.
Deterrents of cost and quality will have to be overcome because of consumer
pressure to make suitable corrugated containers available to the exporting
industries at economic prices. In citrus packaging, it is believed there
will be a strong growth in the need for corrugated containers, particularly
if the government recognizes the implications to the exporters and intro-
duces export packaging standards.
Projected Future Demand
A minimum growth rate expectancy in-corrugated containers in
countries which have reached the degree of development of Turkey is 10% per
year, and it is believed that a realistic growth rate will lie in the range
of 10% to 12Z. Rates of growth in Turkey are forecast at 8% for the "lower
limit" and 10% for the "higher limit" beyond 1982. It is estimated that the
market is undersupplied to the extent of between 25 and 33%; if anything, the
higher figure is likely to be the more realistic.
The anticipated production of linerboard as compared to the
alternative production of sackkraft from the Antalya project has been based
on the following assumptions:



ANNEX 9
Page 11
(a) The project market for linerboard is the average of the high
and low projections contained in the market study;
(b) The Caycuma mill will continue to produce sackkraft at capacity;
(c) Start-up of new facilities is based on the attainment of 50% of
rated capacity in the first year of operation, 71% in the second
year, 86% in the third year, and full production in the fourth
year;
(d) In the early years of operation of the Antalya mill also
substantial amounts of sackkraft will be produced in order
to service this market and to utilize the remaining capacity.
The production of sackkraft is gradually diminishing as
the needs for linerboard increase.
No problems in selling the output of the Antalya mill are foreseen.
If the comparative price structure within the Turkish economy
allows, and the packaging industry requires, a price reduction in liner-
board comparable to imports, the Antalya mill could meet this requirement
if direct taxes in production costs are removed. Under these circumstances
the Antalya mill would be in a position to produce linerboard at the same
price as imports. On this basis, the probable demand in a market where
supplies are readily available under the pricing policies which presently
apply, the forecast below represents the lower and upper limits of growth
In demand.

Sackkraft
Use of Sackkraft
Some 80% of all sackkraft produced is used in Turkey's cement
industry and about 25 cement plants are in operation. The nominal annual
capacity is close to 10 million tons. Cement exports of 1.4 million tons
are subsidized by the Government. Production costs are reportedly higher
than export selling prices. Expansion of the existing plants and the
construction of new ones is expected to increase total capacity of the in-
dustry to 14 million tons by 1975. The growth in cement production will
require a substantial increase in the supply of sackkraft. The standard
cement sack in Turkey is constructed from 3 piles of 70 g/=2 for domestic



PANEX 9
Page 13
2
usage and 6 piles for export usage, 5 piles at 70 g/m2 ar4d 1 ply at 90 gfm
On this basis 150 tons of cement for export or 280 tons of cement for the
domestic market, can be packaged in one ton of paper.  For 1972, the corres-
ponding sackkraft requirements are 9,300 TPY for export packaging and 27,200
TPY for domestic packaging for a total of 36,500 TPY.
The remaining 20% of the country's consumption of sackkraft is
taken up primarily by three other industries:  fertilizer, feed grain, and
gypsum. Of these, fertilizer is the largest consumer of paper. The 1972
production of fertilizer is predicted to be approximately 1.5 million tons.
Expansion now underway will bring the fertilizer capacity to about 4.5
million tons by 1976 and long-term objectives are for 9.7 million tons by
1982.  Although some fertilizer manufacturers use sackkraft for packaging,
there is strong bias in favor of plastic bags.  This preference stems from
the granular and hygroscopic nature of fertilizer which demands a high
degree of moisture resistance from the container. The use of plastic-coated
sackkraft would substantially influence the use of paper packaging for ferti-
lizer in the future. If adopted, the growth in paper for this purpose could
be very large.
Present and Forecasted Situation
The estimated consumption for sackkraft in 1972 is 44,500 TPY
of which 36,500 are consumed by the cement industry. No import needs exist
at present since the Caycuma plant with a capacity of about 60,000 TPY has
reached almost full capacity utilization. Given the reasons above the proj-
ection of future consumption for sackkraft is most closely tied to the ex-
pansion of the cement industry. By 1975, assuming the same operating ratio
as presently exists, cement production should reach 12.5 million tons. If
2 million tons of this are exported, the total sackkraft consumption would
be a minimum of 51,000 TPY. Other industries by 1975 will consume approxi-
mately 11,000 TPY for a total requirement of sackkraft of 62,000 TPY. Ex-
cellent statistics on the production of cement are maintained in Turkey, and
it is possible to use these figures, together with those for capacity under
construction to develop fairly accurate medium term estimates of cement industry
needs up to 1975. Estimates of use outside the cement industry are less
secure, but certainly the rapidly growing fertilizer industry is a natural
competitive area for paper against plastics and a growing share of this
market can be expected.  Consumption in the cement industry is also likely
to be stimulated by an upgrading in packaging specifications which will
require that one ply per sack be 90 g/m2 paper.
Beyond 1975, the projection becomes less certain, being influenced
to a large degree by the government's policy on cement exports. There is
some indication that this may slow down as the countries to which exports are
made become more self-sufficient in cement. The competition from bulk hand-



ANNEX 9
Page 14
Ling of cement does not at this time appear to be a serious threat, but in
view of the experience elsewhere in the world it cannot be completely dis-
regarded. For example, some inquiries are reported for cement exports to the
United States but such shipments would likely be in the form of clinker ce-
ment in bulk carriers.  Based on the above mentioned assumptions, particularly
on cement production, projections of demand for sackkraft up to 1990 are
shown below. The State Planning Office projections have also been included.

The State Planning Organization projects an average growth in cement produc-

Again, as for linerboard, the SPO estimates show a reasonable
relationship with the Sandwell forecasts but at the higher rather than the
lower levels. Year by year projections will tend to be wrong in any one
year because demand will increase in steps related to new capacity in the
cement industry or the use of sackkraft for packaging fertilizers.  The
average annual growth rate assumed from present consumption to 1978 for
the low projection amounts to 10% and 11.5% for the high projection, there-
after it averages to about 8% for both projections. After 1986, a third
sackkraft paper machine may be required. At that point, the Antalya machine
would gradually reduce its sackkraft production and revert to linerboard
exclusively. The proposed Antalya machine would have considerable flexi-
bility,  Additional capacity would be required in 1983 if the market develops
as expected.

Converting Industry
The converting companies are mostly owned by cement producers.
The exception is the plant operated by SEKA at Izmit. At mid-1972, there
were seven sackkraft plants operating or under construction in Turkey; these
include:
SEKA                      at lzmit
Cesan                     near Istanbul
Nuh Cimento               at Hereke (near Istanbul)
Akciiento                 near Istanbul (under construction)
Bartin Cimento Torba S. at Bartin (near Caycuma)
Cimentas                  at Izmir
Adana Torba               at Adana
With the addition of a converting operation at Akeimento's plant
west of Istanbul, and new capacity at Nuh Cimento's plant between Istanbul
and Izmit, there is sufficient sack producing capacity to meet short-term
requirements. Up to now any problems in meeting the needs of the cement
industry have arisen from shortages of paper, not sack converting capacity.
The continuing expansion in cement may justify sack plant installations at
some future time. The only imudiate prospect for a new plant appears to
be the establishment of such a facility in the Ankara area in order to serve
the two cement plants there and plants at Isparta, Konya, Sivas, Corum and
Bolu. These mills are on the Anatolian Plateau and could be serviced quite
logically from an Ankara converting plant.
Sawnwood
The State Planning Office estimates that about 80% of all
sawnwood produced in Turkey is used for building construction, and the
balance for furniture, shipbuilding and other miscellaneous purposes. These
latter uses are usually more demanding in their quality specifications. Con-
struction grades are used largely for non-integrated purposes in Turkey,
as temporary-use naterial necessary for the placing of permanent non-wood
building components. Their integral part use is confined largely to roof
rafters and tile battens. The greater part finds its use in larger and
usually multi-storied structures - apartments, commercial and industrial
buildings.
About 95% of the wood supplied to the Antalya project would be
pinus brutia which is currently regarded as a construction grade only. Turkish
construction traditions and methods ensure a large and continuing market for
construction sawnwood, although some inroads are bound to be made by new
technology in the form of steel scaffolding and supports, and to a lesser
extent by steel and plywood concrete forms.



ANNE 9
Page 16
Construction Grades
The standard construction sawnwood used is concentrate( in three
main nominal classifications as set out below@  No regulations are imposed on
private sector producers as to dimensions or grade, although the slate-owned
mills are moving to grading of softwood sawnwood.
(a) Boards: This grade has a nominal thickness of 2.5 cm .ad is
produced in random widths of from 10 cm to 20 cm with some £nall
proportion up to 25 cm. Lengths range from about 2.5 to 5 t the
most common width being about 15 cm, and tne length about 3 a.
Boards find their use in the construction of concrete forms
where random widths afford flexibility in selection. Short mnd
extra long lengths are less preferred than the middle range 3f 3
to 4.5 m,
(b)  "5 x 10's":  These are nominally of the dimensions notEl, 5 cm
by 10 cm and are used in a variety of applications, as brac 3 where
strengthening is required, as non-integral material in conciate
form construction, in scaffold bracing, and as roof rafters. Many
of their uses require short lengths and there is a tolerancE of
"shorts".
(c)  "10 x 10's":  Nominally of 10 cm by 10 cm dimensions, r e used
extensively as vertical supports to hold up concrete forms i r casting
of floors in multi-story buildings, and as scaffold members   They
are more durable than either boards of 5 x 10's. Common leryths
are 2.5 and 3.0 m.
Discussions with sawmill operators and contractors idi dated that
over 80 percent of all construction sawnwood is sold in the above c Imensions.
Istanbul builders indicated a trend from 10 x 10 to 8 x 8 for shoriig
supports. There are, of course, a number of other classifications Lncluding
planks and battens. Many of these products are custom orders, oftE i sawn
from logs provided by the customer. With the exception of village iousing
all the identifiable applications for construction sawnwood are in irban
areas.
Zoinery Grades
There is no clear-cut line betieen goinery aad construc:ion grades
In zerms of actual application.  Within thc joinery classification :here is
a range of grades at the top of which is the clear grade, that is, iithout
knots or other dowagra&ing characteristics. But much joinery lumbe- .has a
tolarance for small knots whLich are tight. These lowser grades are ised for
purposes wnich do not require exposure to view.



ANNEX 9
Page 17
However, all joinery grades require stability and freedom from
warping. Where there is exposure to weather and to view, joinery grades
must present a good surface in terms of smoothness and receptivity to paint.
In some applications small knots are bored out and replaced with plugs and
filler to provide a good painting surface.
Pinus brutia is not an accepted joinery grade although some mills
producing both joinery and construction grades to infiltrate small quantities
of higher quality brutia into their parcels of joinery sawnwood.
Most of the joinery sawnwood produced in sawmills is cut to large
cross-sectional dimensions as "cants" or "flitches" for remanufacture in
joinery plants. Canta and flitches range upward from 10 ca in thickness
and to 40 cm in width.
Notwithstanding the above mentioned deficiencies of pinus brutia
it has made important inroads into the traditional markets, especially in
Istanbul.
Field interviews in Istanbul indicated that within the last few
years, Pinus brutia has taken an increasing share of the construction grade
market. As late as 1966 less than 10 percent of all construction sawnwood
was in the pine classification, primarily Scots pine, but a small amount of
Pinua brutia. It is estimated that in 1972, about 65 percent of the consump-
tion was pine, with 60 percent of that proportion, about 40 percent of all
construction sawnwood, being Pinus brutia.
This increased acceptance has been won through pricing policies.
The indicated differential between Pinus brutia and other coniferous
construction grades was reported to be about 20 to 25 percent. Rapidly
rising prices for all grades have led users to substitute lower quality
sawnwood and this has provided sales opportunities for Pinus brutia. No
problems should be encountered in selling if the price differential is
maintained.
Sawnwood Consumption in the Antalya Market Area
There is no long-term statistical record of the regional consump-
tion of sawnwood. Only within the last 18 months has the Government pub-
lished monthly statistics of the building permits issued for new construction
on a regional basis. During the 10 months period for which detailed statis-
tics were available, June 1971 to March 1972, an analysis was made using floor
area as an indicator of sawnwood usage. The analysis of new construction
during the period of detailed reporting indicates that about 54% of the floor
area was constructed in six marketing areas which would logically be supplied
with sawnwood produced in an Antalya complex.

In addition to these prime marketing areas, there are tributary
areas which could be serviced from Antalya. These include the areas contig-
uous to Istanbul on the European side of the Bosphorus, Kocaeli Province to
the east which could be serviced by water, and Bursa, Eskisehir, Afyon,
Denizli, Aydin and Hatay Province which could be serviced by road. The
inclusion of these tributary areas raises the accessible market area to about
60 percent of the new construction recorded in the 10-month period.
Although it is believed that there is a large concentration of new
construction within the marketing areas itemized on the preceding page, it
appears unsound to believe that 54% of the Turkish sawnwood market lies in
the six prime marketing areas.
Undoubtedly they are the dominant regions and are experiencing
population growth at rates in excess of the national average. However, less
than one-third of Turkey's population of almost 36,000,000 resided in the
marketing area in 1970.
Also taking into account the distribution of population, it is
estimated that a more rational estimate of the sawnwood consumption within
the logical marketing area of Antalya is between 40 and 45% of the national
total.  If the higher of these figures is accepted and applied 3to the FAO
estimates of a total sawnwood consumption of about 2,256,030  3 in 1970, the
regional market in that year was slightl  over 1,000,000 m  and by 1972 was
in the order of 1,100,000 to 1,200,000 m , The application of a ratio of 2:1
for t,he proportion of construction grades to .oinery grades gives an indicated
current consumption of between 725,000 and 800,00G m3 of construction sawuwood
used in the regional market, and between 300,000 to 400,000 m3 of joinery
grades.

Prices for Aftalya Products
Linerboard and Sackkraft
Paper prices in Turkey are set by the Government. 1/ Since July
1971,, the price of sackkraft and linerboard FOB the Caycuma Mill of SEKA was
5,100 TL/ton. 2/ Delivery is taken by the customer at the mill with the
customer arranging transportation and paying all freight charges. This
same price would apply to a mill in Antalya. Test or bogus linerboard sells
for 4,000 TL/ton while bogus or straw corrugating medium (180 g/m2) sells for
about 3,000 TL/ton.
A price history' of linerboard and sackkraft as well as corrugating
medium in Turkey does not have too much significance in view of the 67% TL
devaluation of August 1970. From 1965 through mid-1971 linerboard sold at
the same price of 4,400 TL/ton, equivalent to $490/ton at the exchange range
which applied prior to devaluation. In mid-1971 the Government authorized an
increase to the current price level of 5,100 TL/ton, equivalent to $365 at
the current rate. During the same span of years the price of corrugating
medium (straw board) increased from 1,650 TL/ton to the current 3,000 TL/ton.
In view of rising wood prices and inflationary trends, it is believed that
further price increases are likely to occur in the near future.
The practice of selling both linerboard and sackkraft at the same
price is contrary to normal international pricing where sackkraft is usually
20 to 40% more expensive than linerboard due to lower pulp yields, lower
machine output, and higher iabor and:other Costs per ton of product.
Import Policy and Effects of Common Market
Restraints on paper imports include non-monetary impediments in
the form of applications, approvals, foreign exchange allocations and a clear-
ance from SEKA, as well as import duties, taxes and other landing costs.
Government policy has been consistent in encouraging the highest
degree of self-sufficiency in pulp and paper in order to utilize to the
greatest degree of both human and natural resources despite sometimes high
internal costs.
1/  For a discussion of Turkish prices compared with World prices, see Annex T1-3
2/   The price of linerboard was raised to 6750 TL/ton in September 1973, but
the increase has not been taken into account in this appraisal.

In addition to the above direct charges, there is a bank financing
charge of 12 percent. Duty, Municipal Tax and Harbor costs of together
47.3% exclusively apply to imports whereas the other charges also apply to
domestic production.
Under legislation enacted in January 1972, the Government granted to
SEKA the exclusive right to withdraw imports of paper from customs without
payment of any of the foregoing charges, and authority to resell such imports
at domestic price levels. This permitted SEKA to generate a substantial profit
in 1971 on these transactions. No immediate change in the present policy with
respect to imports, import mechanics, tariffs and taxes appears likely.
Under the agreements with the Common Market, Turkey would attain
full membership by 1992 at which time her tariff structure would conform
to that of the other members. During the intervening years to 1992, tariffs
would be progressively lowered to provide a period of adjustment.
Tariffs on most paper products vis-a-vis the Common Market would
be reduced on a gradual basis as set out in the Table below:

,eansonal variations,

The sawnwood prices on the preceding page could be understated by
as much as 10 or 20% because sawlog prices in 1972 have increased to an
all time high. Several months have passed between the purchase of sawlogs
and the selling of the finished product. Allowing for some log storage,
transportation to the mill, and air drying it is possible to visualize at
least a three months delay before sawnwood is finally sold on the market.



ANNEX 9
Page 24
Under the present rapidly escalating price of sawnwood any delay from the
time of log purchase to the time of sawnwood sales contributes to profit.
Thus the prices indicated on the preceding pages are believed to be typical
market prices in mid-1972, but could be low when related to the log prices
prevailing in the same period.
Future prices are expected to increase because of strong demands
originating from investments in new and improved housing, industrial and
hotel construction.
Export Market Potential
Though it is expected that domestic demand will absorb production
of paper products from Antalya, the potential export markets were studied by
Sandwell by sampling four eastern Mediterranean countries (Syria, Lebanon,
Israel and Cyprus). The findings on the different products are as follows:
Linerboard - In the absence of any major production in the sample
countries practically all linerboard is imported from North America and
Scandanavia. The established position of present suppliers and their high
standards of quality and service make these markets difficult to penetrate.
However, the net mill prices to suppliers are not as attractive as in other
markets and the improving market conditions in the United States and Western
Europe which are now evident will make these markets less important to
international suppliers.  Turkey's contiguous location offers advantages, but
becomThg established in the market may require some price discounts, justifi-
able in the eyes of buyers because of the shorter freight haul. Therefore
with s-ome marketing efforts an export position could be built-up and liner-
board tot needed in Turkey could provide foreign exchange and at the same time
be financially attractive to the Antalya project.
Sackkraft - A decline in the rate of growth of sackkraft market
is noted in_the sample countries, due in part to the increased bulk handling
of cement.  This, coupled with the very high standards of paper quality and
market servicing presently provided by Scandinavian'suppliers, make sackkraft
a more difficult product to export from Antalya.
Sawnwood - Due to the deficiencies of Pinus brutia as compared to
other wood species, the export opportunities for the most important part of
the project's sawnwood production would be difficult. Additionally, any
effort to penetrate foreign markets would require a revision of quality and
service standards. A large, modern mill of the type visualized for Antalya
should be able to achieve the required standards. Opportunities to export
good quality cedar are excellent but for other species, especially Pinus
brutia, there appears to be little potential.



ANNEX 9
Page 25
The competitive situation in Lebanon, presently dominated by two
East European countries (mainly Romania) is difficult. They have centrally
managed economies which are integrated with national commercial goals. These
broader objectives seem to be pointed up in the willingness of Russian sup-
pliers to cut prices to maintain exports. However, ir. view of increasing
demand in other countries, Antalya with its modern large production facilities
and located near the coast, would be in a good position to achieve marketing
and distribution economics.
C. Marketing for Antalya Products
In view of the near monopoly which SEKA holds in the supply of liner-
board and corrugating medium, the Company's absolute monopoly in the supply of
domestic sackkraft, its exclusive right of importation of industrial papers,
and a chronic under-supply situation, it is therefore understandable that
selling conditions can easily be set by SEKA without giving much consideration
to the special needs of the purchasers. Among the practices followed by SEKA
which would not exist under competitive conditions are the following:
(a) SEKA sells most of its pulp and paper prepaid at its
warehouse door. It is the customers responsibility to arrange
for transportation and to pay all freight charges;
(b) Each converter must submit to SEKA before year end his annual
requirements of linerboard and corrugating medium for the following
year. This is unknown in a competitive system, but understandable
where limited manufacturing capacity is available and quotas may
have to be established;
(c) Payment of progressive deposits ensures the product is fully
paid for on completion of the production run. For example, for
linerboard a deposit amounting to about 5% of the value of the order
must be deposited with SEKA at the time orders are placed. From
time to time during the operating year SEKA advises the converter
when a program will be run to supply his needs and a further deposit
of 25% of the order value must be made. When the periodic order is
actually run, the converter is advised and must deposit 75% balance
of the order value with SEKA before delivery. No credit terms apply.
At the end of each year an adjustment is made for the small earnest
deposit which was made when annual requirements were indicated;
(d) The converter, when placing his periodic order must accept the
full reel width of the machine which SEKA assigns for its production.
Inasmuch as the converter must produce containers to the size
specifications of his customers, he may be required to order roll
sizes from SEKA which, at the order, he cannot use;
(e) SEKA does not provide assistance and coordination to converters.



ANNEX 9
Page 26
Both the physical and financial burdens imposed upon the converter
by the practices set down above create additional costs for linerboard or
corrugating medium at the converting plant. The continuation of this policy
has to be questionied. The Antalya mill will provide large quantities of
linerboard for the domestic market thereby providing improved packaging for
domestically consumed and export goods.  It is in the country's interest
that the distribution costs for paper products in general are minimized. It
is also in SEKA's interest so that it will be able to sell its products in
a balanced market. For linerboard, an equilibrium between domestic supply
and demand is expected at least during the early operating years of the
Antalya project. Therefore, SEKA has to develop some marketing efforts
and start to turn from a production-oriented to a market-oriented company.
The introduction of a marketing concept for linerboard would at the same
time provide an excellent start for the other paper products. Given Turkey's
gradual exposure to international markets with decreasing import duties
would require a change of SEKA's attitude to marketing in any case. Therefore,
it is recommended that SEKA undertake a study how to establish a marketing
system which serves sellers and purehasers needs as well. In this study
particularly the following aspects should be emphasized:
a)   Evaluate the relative prices of linerboard, corrugating medium, and
sack-kraft to ensure that the relationship is based on equitable
differences in costs of production including all direct and indirect
charges associated with manufacture.
b)   Change the prior deposit requirements as these indirectly increase
the price to customers in the sense that their working capital is
increased. If the supplemental income derived from the use of the
deposits is necessary for SEKA, a more equitable and less complex
method would be to increase prices to the extent necessary and fol-
low more usual commercial selling terms. This could mean cash on
delivery or the extension of credit for a specified period to cus-
tomers of proven integrity.
c)   Change the full-trim practice which requires a buyer to purchase the
full reel width even if side-runs are not of use to him. It is re-
ported that SEKA presently programs reels on a single customer basis.
It is recommended instead that orders of the same basis weight and
furnish specification be grouped, and machines trimmed to obtain
maximum output.
d)   Prior ordering over a period of a year should be terminated.  Some
indication of annual needs should be obtained from customers, but
lead time on order specifications should not exceed 2 months.



ANNEX 9
Page 27
e)   Provision should be made for technical servicing of accounts to
provide converters with all assistance possible in making their opera-
tions efficient, in resolving production problems, and in the design-
ing and setting of performance standards for containers. The partici-
pation of SEKA in the converting business provides a technical base
for this service.
f)   The use of a "delivered cost" pricing system would permit more rational
delivery schedules and avoid the congestion and the lost waiting time
for trucks which characterizes many of the industrial operations in
Turkey. In the event that barge transportation was established to
service the proposed mill, base-point pricing policies should be followed,
with prices quoted FOB discharge depots at Istanbul, Izmir and Mersin.
Marketing System for Sawnwood
The Antalya sawmill with an annual output of 182,000 m3 sawnwood
will be by far the largest sawmill in Turkey. Therefore, the marketing of its
sawnwood has to be carefully planned; this is not so much because it may be
needed not only to sell the output without major marketing efforts, but also
to ensure an organic evolution of the existing sawmilling industry.
Selling Practices
The existence of two areas of production, the public and private
sectors, is reflected in selling methods. Generally the public mills are
located close to the sources of logs, the private mills close to the con-
suming markets.
The selling practices presently applied are as follows:
Public Sector
All sales are made at the producing mill under a system of competitive
bidding. Periodic auctions are held 4 to 12 times a year, depending on mill
size, with prior public and mailed arnnouncements being made to potential buyers.
The parcels of sawnwood are usually of mixed dimensions, but grade and volume
are itemized. Buyers interested in a particular parcel must make a prior deposit
of 7-1/2 percent of the upset price as evidence of good faith. If successful in
the oral auction on the day of sale an additional 7-1/2 percent is paid and an
interim sale agreement is signed subject to approval by the Ministry in Ankara.
Upon taking possession the buyer is required to pay the full 100 percent of pur-
chase price, as well as some small local taxes. He must also arrange and pay
for loading and transport to his destination.
Private Sector
Such complexity of purchasing mechanics do not apply in the private
sector. The producing mills are customarily close to the consuming area and
the buyer makes his purchase, usually after some bargaining, and takes imme-
diate possession upon payment. Unless the buyer elects to "shop around" among



AINNEX 9
Page 28
other producers in the area his purchase is usually completed within a few
hours.
Some of the large private mills have a clientele of buyers which
are usually larger contractors who wish to avoid the administrative costs
of attending auctiorns o-- buying from smaller mills,  Established relationships,
integrity and reliabilitv of supply lead such buyers to obtain their suppliers
from larger mills even at the cost of having longer freight hauls.
The Antalya mill should avoid involvement in the retail distribution
of sawnwood to a wide array of small buyers in order to confine its efforts
largely to the production side, and to sales in large parcels for distribution
by specialists in sawnwood and building supplies. This policy would be rein-
forced if it were decided to ship large cargos of sawnwood to Istanbul and
Izmir by barge.
Under a wholesale system the following mechanics of operation could
apply:
-    Accredited wholesalers would be appointed in each of the
marketing areas to merchandise the mill's sawnwood products.
This would not be an exclusive regional agency, but the,
number of wholesalers handling the company's production would
be limited.
-    Wholesalers would be required to carry stocks to serve their
customers. They would order these stocks on a periodic ba-
sis through the producing company's representative in the
local area. In the six prime marketing areas the sales func-
tion would be closely integrated with the transport function,
the operations being consolidated in the same office.
-    Large contractors would be granted wholesale price privileges,
provided their annual volume of purchases attained certain
agreed levels.
-    Prices would be announced on a periodic basis as is done by
SEKA for its paper sales, and by the Demirel sawmill.
-    A sales commission of 7-1/2 percent would be provided to
wholesalers to cover their distributiorn costs, working
capital, and a reasonable profit margin.
-    All sales to 4holesalers or contractors would be made against
orders filed with the regional sales office.
-    All sawnwood would be graded anr  marked in conformity with
the standards employed by the State mills.



ANNEX 9
Page 29
Sawnwood would be sold in packets as is done by the State
mills. However, each packet would contain a common dimension
(except for Boards where random widths would apply), and a
common grade. Packets would be bundled to permit easy
handling in loading, unloading, and reloading at receiving
ports if a tug and barge system were used. This type of
packaging does not preclude the local distributor from break-
ing bundles on trucks to permit unloading by hand.
Close liaison would be maintained among the mill production
and sales staff, local sales office, transportation staff,
and wholesalers to coordinate the production and selling
functions, especially as this relates to the marketing of
those specific grades and dimensions which best accommodate
efficient mill operations.
The system suggested does not provide for the company
carrying inventory,stocks in local marketing areas.  If
tug and barge transport were used, all shipments would be
made against specific orders for immediate forwarding
to the wholesalers or large buyers. Some inventory would be
carried at the mill to service wholesalers efficiently.
It is recommended that SEKA undertake a study on the marketing of
sawnwood considering the existing selling practices, the above proposal and
their own field investigation and paying particular attention to the implica-
tions for the existing sawmill industry. Given the large impact of the proposed
selling practice for sawnwood, this study should then be reviewed with the
Turkish Authorities and the Bank before a final choice on the selling and dis-
tribution practice is made.
Regional Market Sales
Based on the regional market forecast and detailed marketing consid-
erations the following general sales targets are foreseen for the Antalya
mill:
Market Sales Targets
Percent of
Marketing Area       Sales Volume                Total
m3/A
Istanbul-Kocaeli      92,000                      50
Ankara                36,000                      20
Izmir                 18,000                      10
Mersin-Adana           9,000                       5
Konya                  9,000                       5
Antalya-South Coast  18,000                       10
Total            182,000                     100



iNNEX 9
Page 30
The Istanbul area will be the dominant market area for several reasons:
a)   Its large population - 3,000,000 persons in 1970 - and
rapid growth - an increase of 30% over 1965;
b)   Its accessibility by water and the ability to service
this market efficiently;
c)   Its acceptance of Pinus brutia as a construction grade
if prices offer some advantage over other species.
In suggesting market targets, Ankara has been given a much lower
weight than Istanbul for reasons of the latter's absolute size and growth,
and the ready accessibility of this market to producers in the Bolu-Kastamonu
areas which is less than 250 km away. This compares with a distance of 600
km from a potential site at Manavgat. A larger share of the Ankara market
could be attained if there were an embargo on the export of logs from the
Antalya resource area to sawmills in the Ankara area. In that situation
those mills would have to turn north for their supplies and the increased
competition at auctions would increase prices still further to the benefit
of the Antalya area mill.
Izmir is not only a smaller market but is accessible to producers in
the western basmudurluk. It has the advantage of being reachable by water,
but its population and growth rate are both lower than Ankara.
The Konya marketing area embraces a wide basmudurluk in which the
population of the city proper is only 25% of the marketing region it services.
A major deterrent to penetration of this market by an Antalya mill is the poor
condition of Highway 31 north from Manavgat.
The Antalya basmudurluk market extending along the south coast to
Alanya is a natural selling area made more attractive by the anticipated rapid
growth in the tourism industry.  The freezing of log sales in Antalya would
limit private mill production to existing levels, permitting the new proposed
mill to service the growth market.
The Mersin-Adana basmudurluk is also a growing market, but not of
suci size as to permit capitalizing upon its accessibility by water. Truck
transport over existing roads is costly, and since the basmudurluk has a well-
established sawmilling industry and a resource base which permits expansion,
this market has been accorded a lesser degree of attraction as a potential
merchandizing area.

ANTALYA FOREST UTILIZATION PROJECT
STATE INVESTMENT BANK, TURKEY
Introduction
The Bank sent a mission (Colin M. Southall and Edgar C.H. Su) to
Turkey in April, 1973 to conduct a reviewcf the organization, policies,
procedures, operations, and financial position of the State Investment
Bank (SIB), with a view to exploring the possibility of using SIB as a inter-
mediary for the Bank loan for the Antalya Forest Utilization Project. The
mission was also asked to form an opinion on whether a long-term institut-
ional relationship could be developed with SIB for channeling Bank funds
for other Turkish public sector projects in the future. The mission returned
with favorable impressions of the institution. It was felt that SIB, despite
its present weaknesses, would be able to undertake the task related to the
Antalya Project and to make necessary improvements which should precede a
long-term partnership with the Bank. Based upon the recommendations of the
mission, the Bank has decided to use SIB as an intermediary for channeling
the Bank loan for the Antalya Project. Regarding the establishment of a
long-term relationship with SIB, prospects appear good although final
decision will be made only after further work has been done by SIB and in
the Bank, and after a full appraisal of the institution has been completed.
The recent mission's findings and conclusions are summarized below.
Establishment of SIB - Objectives
SIB was created by Law 441 promulgated on March 12, 1964 to provide
credits and guarantees to State Economic Enterprises (SEEs) for their invest-
ments. The purpose was to ensure that the scarce resources available for
the development of public sector projects would be put to use for projects
which were technically, financially and economically sound. Before the SIB
was established, the long-term credit needs of the SEEs were met by the
Amortization and Credit Fund which acted merely as a disbursement agent with
no authority and manpower to appraise projects. SIB is the first financial
institution ever established in Turkey which channels funds to public sector
projects on the basis of the results of appraisals, instead of on instruc-
tions from a Ministry.
SIB's initial authorized share capital was LT 1,000 million but
this was doubled in 1972. As of December 31, 1972, SIB's paid-up share
capital amounted to LT 1,023 million.
Organization
SIB has a Board of five Directors. Three ex-officio members,
namely Mr. Nedim Rustu Aksal, the General Manager of SIB, and Mr. Tarik Kivane
and Mr. Zeki Dusluoglu who are Deputy General Managers. Two others are
appointed by the Ministry of Finance the the CouniiI of Ministers respec-
tively, namely Mr. Helmet Turat, former General Director of DSI, and
Mr. Mehmet Ertugruloglu, former General Director of the Amortization and
Credit Fund. The Board meets once a week. All projects to be financed



A>mE X 12
Page 2
by SIB require the Board's approval, but before they are presented to the
Board, they are subject to clearance by tile Credlt Committee.  Since the
Board is controlled by its ex-officio members, Board approval is assured if
the senior management of SIB supports a proposal.
Mr. Aksal has held a number of Important Government posts and is
able to deal on an equal basis with his counterparts in o.-' "r SEEs. lIe has
been the Gerneral Manager of SIB since 196&-, and it is he wao has been largely
responsible for building up a qualified and capable staff i'L' SIB and in
improving its procedures.
The two Deputy General Managers have clearly defined responsibili-
ties each supervising several departments. H r. Dusluoglu is in charge of
raising resources, programming, loan admiriistration, accounting and disbur-
sements. Mr. Kivanc is responsible for the financial, economic and technical
appraisal of all projects submitted to SIB for financing and for tide super-
vision of the projects until they have been implemented. Both are well
qualified and competent.
At present SIB has a staff of 110 including 55 professionals, the
distribution of which is set forth in the organizational chart. However, SIB
has an authorized staff of 181, and if its role were enlarged, an additional
71 staff members could be recruited without any additional authorization
from the Government. Each year SIB advertises for the professional staff it
requires and around 200 applicants for employment are received. 1/ The most
promising applicants are given a written examination and of the top 15 not
more than 5 are offered positions in SIB. As a result, the staff recruited
by SIB are well qualified and the turnover of staff is not high. Although
SIB staff receive at least twice the salary received by their counterparts
in other Government Departments, because they can be employed on a contract
basis rather than in accordance with the provisions of the Personnel Law,
the ratio of SIB's administrative expenses to its total assets is below 1%.

SIB has no written policy statement dealing with investment and
ort olio management. Since its financing of SEEs' projects is on a con-
tinu'ing basis, SIB is unable to set any limit to its exposure in a single
o or establiskhment. Nor has SIB fixed the minimum amount for which it
VL_l consider making a credit, although it has never financed any projects
of a very small size. Neither is there any limitation on SIB's own borrow-
ing power.
Procedures
Project Appraisal
The long-term credit needs of the SEEs were met by the Amortiza-
tion and Credit Fund u7ntil the establishment of the SIB.  No project apprai-
sal work was done as this was not required under the Statutes of the Fund,
and its operations were limited to the disbursement of credits within
approved limits. In tihe first year, SIB recruited 22 experts to undertake
thle project appraisal work.  By now, the number has reached 31, including
a engineers, 12 financial analysts and 11 economists. Thirteen of the
appraisal staff have been trained either in the United States or in Europe.
Du-ring the past 9 years , SIB has received 597 applications. After
t:. zin ~-ar'J review,fi, `.0  applicatioas were returned to the applicant SEEs for
',... .hoe principal reasona we-a the inadequacy of credit limits,
', oIlack of financial vility.  The remaining 496 projects
.'. aW ad app-zf.ova   of wAhich 177 were new projects and 319 continuing
._S,c -Z s    .-- contcinu'n-z  -rojects, SIB's apraisal work appears to consist
awy _n cnecx ng, u-datiag, and supplaeenting the work done by the SEEs

ANNEX 12
Page 5
For relatively large projects, SIB's engineers have relied on the
SEE's own judgment on the pricing of the equipment to be procured. SIB has
not established any guidelines concerning the procurement procedures to be
followed by its borrowers. Its economic analysis includes the calculation
of the value added of the project, capital/output ratio, capital/employment
ratio, the effects on the balance of payments, etc. SIB's assessment of the
international competitiveness of projects evaluated by it needs to be
strengthened.
SIB's appraisal staff are qualified people, with the capacity to
appraise industrial projects. They have been continually improving the
quality of their appraisals. During the past two years, considerable progress
has been made as reflected in their appraisal reports. SIB has also organized
seminars on project appraisal for the past five years, for the benefit of
the staff of SEE's. Such seminars have been found useful, especially by
those SEE's whose project appraisal work needs to be up-graded.
Follow-up
SIB has been making serious efforts to follow-up the implementation
of projects financed by it.   Its engineers and financial analysts visit
SIB-financed projects regularly. SIB's follow-up work terminates at the
time when a project is completed, in accordance with its Statutes. Disburse-
ments are made only after the engineers are satisfied with the progress
being made, and the borrower's accounts are examined by SIB's financial
analysts to ensure that all expenditures have been made properly for the
goods and services required for the project.
Reporting from clients is generally inadequate. Since the SEEs
are required to submit reports to different agencies of the Government, they
usually send copies of their standard reports to SIB which are not very
useful. Therefore, SIB attached great importance to staff visits to pro-
jects and to the reports prepared by these staff on their return.
Disbursement and Collection Procedures
SIB has been following the established disbursement and collection
procedures, as briefly described below, which are adequate. During the
past year the new manager in charge of disbursements and collections has
made some changes which increased the efficiency of both. Withdrawals are
made on the basis of invoices and other supporting documents submitted by
the borrowers, after they have been checked by the technical staff. In most
cases they are reimbursements. Advance payments are made only in a limited
number of cases when this is justified. On the whole, the disbursement
procedures appear to be successful in ensuring that SIB loans are actually
used for the related projects.
Each borrower is put on notice fifteen days before an installment
is due. When payment is not made on the due date, an entry is immediately



ANNEX 1 2
Page 6
made ir the arrears statement wh,Iieh is made available to the top management
daily. Loans made during the last two years have been repaid promptly.
The Credits Division which is also in charge of disbursements now
has a staff of 15, including one manager, one assistant manager, two senior
officers, 9 officers and two typists. If SIB is used as an intermediary
for channelling foreign ex&caange resources from IBRD and/or EIB, two officers
in the Resources Secti.3n who have good command of the English language, will
be transferred to the Credit-s Division and together with some of the Division's
existing staff will be assigned to handLe foreign exchange transactions. On
a rotationaL basis, the disbursement staff will receive training in the Foreign
Exchange Department of Central Bank for a period of two to three months.
Given the present work load, the disbursement officers are carrying
work efficiently. However, further training will be necessary, if an addit-
ional assignment is taken up. The arrangements which have been made and are
to be made to enable the staff to get more acquainted with the procedures
related to disbursements of foreign funds appear to be satisfactory.
Resources
SIB's resources include share capital paid up by the Government,
the proceeds of tax free bond issues guaranteed by the Government which are
sold to the Pension Fund and Social Insurance Corporation, funds taken over
from the Amortization and Credit Fund and, the Internal Financing Fund and
Treasury advances. In addition, the Ministry of Finance has been making
working capital loans through SIB to the SEEs on an administered basis.
The resources raised by SIB since its inception are shown in
Table I.  As of the end of 1972, SIB had obtained resources totalling
L: 13,767 million (US$983 million equivalent), of which 90% was from ex-
ternal sources, mainly by selling bonds to the State Social Insurance and
Peasion Funds. The bonds issued recently have a term of 20 years and bear
interest at 9% p.a. (tax free). The only foreign exchange funds are the
foreign debt taken over by SIB from the Internal Financing Fund in 1964,
for which SIB has assumed the foreign exchange risk by order of the Government.
As a result of the Lira devaluation in August 1970, SIB has incurred con-
siderable losses. (The Government did not compensate SIB for such losses).
the end cf 1972, tne outstanding foreign debt amounted to approximately
42  lLo   equivalent.

The amortization period of SIB's long-term loans ranged from 5 to
over 20 years.  Seventy-one percent of the loans extended by SIB during the
Second Five-Year Plan period (1968-1972) are for more than ten years and
the average size of its loans was LT 24 million. In 1972 the total amount
of loans of LT 100 million or more exceeded 79% of the total amount of all
loans.
Since 1971, the interest rates charged on Sib's loans have ranged
from 9% to 10.5%. The interest rate for each loan is determined by SIB's
Board (within the 9-10.5% range) on the basis of the cash flow projections
o: the project being considered. As a result, some of the better projects,
have to pay interest at a higher rate because the risk factor has not been
taken into consideration.
In 1966, SIB began to make working capital loans to meet the asort-
termn and seasonal needs of SEEs.  In the following year, as the demand in-
creased to a level i&idi could not be entirely met by SIB, the Government
started to make short-term funds available to SIB for relending to SEEs on
a     :':.ssion basis.  In 1969, tne working capital loans of the two categories
:ctallf LT 1 537 milion.  Since 1970, SIB has discontinued making short-
tarm loans out of its o.:n resources, and all such loans have been made
a.inst Gove-zL.ent funds and on a commission $3asis.
SIB's lending is not as well diversified as one would expect from
a 6evelop.t boank, -7in view of its clients being limited to SEEs and their
sabl.shnents.  (Also, since 3IB will only finance viable projects that have
been well ?repared, less than 30% of the SEEs have received SIB credits).

In 1972, 25% of the SIB's loans were extended to the non-ferrous metal iidustry
and 21% of the iron and steel industry. Five sectors: chemicals, petroleum,
cement, steel and non-ferrous metals, absorbed 81% of SIB's financing in
1972. The sectoral distribution of SIB's lending for the past five years,
1968-1972, is shown in Table III.

Financial Performance and Condition
SIB's profit before corporate tax doubled during the five years
1965-1969 from LT 103.3 million in 1965 to LT 221.5 million in 1969. (SIB,
like other enterprises, is obligated to pay the corporate tax). In 1970,
a loss of LT 208.8 million was incurred as a result of the Lira devaluation.
in 1971, a profit of LT 266.9 million was realized and 1972 was also a profit-
able year; Ishe unaudivdi ;rofit and loss statement showing a profit before
i-ax of LT 247 million, representing a return of 22% on average equity.
As or the end of 1970, SIB's long-term debts amounted to LT 9.7
billion, corresponding to 9.4 times of its equity. To reduce this ratio,
the authorized share capital was doubled in 1972 from LT 1 billion to
LT 2 billioni, so that SIB could "re-establish an acceptable balance between
external and own resourcest.
SIB's unaudited Profit and Loss Statement for 1972 and Balance
Sheet as of December 31, 1972 are given on pages 11 and 12.
SIB's statutes provide that 15% of its annual net profit is to be
set aside as provisions for possible losses, until this amount equals fifty
percent of its share capital. After SIB's capital has been fully paid-up
ten percent of its annual net profit will be allocated as additional reserve.
In addition for many years SIB's management has set aside 1% of loans out-
standing as extraordinary reserves and this rate was increased to 2% in
1972. After all the appropriations, the remaining surplus is transferred to
the Treasury. This is because the SEEs including the SIB are not permitted
to retain their unappropriated earnings and funds needed by them for new
investments must be provided for in their annual investment and financial
programs. Since SIB's increased share capital has not yet been fully paid-
up, permission has been given for SIB's net earnings after taxes and pro-
visions for the next three or four years to be converted into paid-up share
capital.
The average collection rate (i.e. the amount collected as a per-
centage of total instalments due in one year) during 1967-1969, was around
80%. In 1970, it dropped to 70% reflecting the fact that the liquidity
of some of its client SEEs worsened as a result of the Lira devaluation. In
17?1  it dropped further to 60%.  However, the situation showed considerable
iamrovzment in 1972 and at the end of March !972, arrears of iaterest
smounted Lo iT 251 miPlion and arrears of principal amounted to LT 464
'Ilion w'-ict. represents only 4.2% of its portfolio.  And by the end of 1972,
total arrears of interest had dropped to Ls 151 million and arrears of
prLncipal to LL 236 mill ion, representing 1..9% of its portfolio.  At the
end of 1972, SIB's provisions and reserves totalled LT 304.2 million, rep-
resenting 2.7% of SIB's loan portfolio including working capital loans.

ANNEX 12
Page 14
Conclusions and Recommendations
SIB has been playing an increasing important role in providing
long-term finance needed by the State Economic Enterprises. Its contri-
bution has exceeded 20 percent of the total investments in the SEEs during
the past. In the current year, SIB is expected to provide LT 4.2 billion
against a total requirement of LT 13.7 billion by the SEEs. While there is
room for improvemen. in SIB's organization, policies and procedures, we
consider the institution: (a) to be generally sound given the constraints
faced by the public sector entities in Turkey; (b) to offer good prospects
for establishing in the short run a partner with the World Bank for the
identification and preparation of projects in the public sector and, possibly,
to exercise influence on the reform of public sector enterprises; and (c)
to possess the potential to be developed in the long run as a World Bank
intermediary for channeling funds to public sector enterprises.
SIB's association in the Antalya Forest Utilization Project has to
be viewed in light of the World Bank's long-term objectives in the industrial
public sector. The results of the 1972 industrial project identification
mission have been somewhat disappointing in number and readiness of projects
and it is obvious that the Government has not yet taken the initiatives
needed to support, through adequate project preparation, the major indust-
tialization objective of the new 5-year Plan in the public sector.  The Bank
therefore needs a "correspondent" in Turkey which could promote its views,
help influence industrial policy and identify and prepare projects in the
public sector, and keep its industrial pipeline stocked. Furthermore, if
SIB's appraisal and supervision capabilities were expanded, it could be used
to shoulder some of the tasks assumed hitherto by the Bank and to channel Bank
funds either as an intermediary for individual projects or, if the necessary
policy framework has been established, as a public sector DFC. While this
last objective may ultimately be the most important one, the Bank's immediate
needs, namely in project preparation and in the reform of public sector enter-
prises, warrant by themselves a close association with SIB.
The Antalya project is the only opportunity immediately available
for establishing such an association. While offering specific but limited
advantages as training grounds for disbursements, bookkeeping and supervision
procedures, the project would suffer from equally specific but limited draw-
in terms of the complication of lending and disbursement arrangements.
Thae de-ermining factor in our recommendation to use SIB as a channel for
Baaik funds for Antalya is the indispensable need to accustom the staff of Bank
-a  SIB to  or  togethrandtoreconcie their respective procedures and thus
to establish ehe relationship needed to explore the possibility of a closer
partnership. In the light of the Bank's experience in Turkey in recent years
such a partnership would in all likelihood not develop unless a narrower
association was used as beachhead.
In order to ensure that SIB will be able to act as the Bank's inter-
mediary for the Antalya Forest Utilization project, we suggest that the
following steps be taken by SIB, namely:



ANNEX 12
Page 15
1.   To strengthen the disbursement division
SIB should train or recruit disbursement officers who are
experienced in dealing with foreign exchange transactions. SIB
has indicated to the mission that it has made the necessary
arrangements to have their staff trained in the Central Bank
for a period of three months. In addition, SIB has also approached
TSKB for such training, and if necessary TSKB would be requested
to send its Chief Disbursement Officer in the Foreign Currencies
Department to SIB on a loan basis to train SIB staff in regard to
World Bank loan transactions.
2. To strengthen the bookkeeping and reporting system
SIB should make an effort to strengthen its bookkeeping and
reporting system. Administered funds should be treated separately
both in its books and in its reports. The financial reports of
SIB, now prepared annually, should be prepared quarterly. Prefer-
ably, a Special Account should be established in the existing
system for the Antalya Forest Utilization project funds so that no
commingling with other funds should take place, i.e. all foreign
exchange transactions should be segregated from local currency
transactions, both in recording and in the presentation of reports.
In the longer run SIB should strengthen its project appraisal and
supervision staff. SIB's supervision work appears to have been adequate
during 1972, the last year of the Seconxd-Fve-Year Plan. This year, SIB's
financing is expected to double and the number of projects to be appraised
will increase proportionally. It is necessary that SIB should strengthen
its appraisal and supervision staff by employing additional engineers and
financial analysts, and increasing the amount of supervision given to SIB
projects.

ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
A.   Methodology
To quantify the economic impact of the project, the forestry and
industrial parts are combined, as they are parts of an integrated project;
one part dependent on the other. The economic costs and benefits are;
therefore, the incremental costs and benefits of the integrated project.
The economic costs and benefits are evaluated at world prices.
Goods are divided into tradeables and non-tradeables. Tradeables are valued
at border prices. Non-tradeables--i.e. goods whose domestic production cost
lies between c.i.f. and f.o.b. prices--are valued at the social costs of the
inputs required to produce them, because we estimate that an increase in
demand will cause a corresponding increase in supply, leaving relative prices
in economy unchanged. Both skilled and unskilled labor are valued at their
respective nominal wage rates, because we estimate that the rapid increase in
agricultural and tourism activities in the project area are expected to absorb
the unemployed labor during most of the project lifetime. Duties, taxes and
other transfer payments are eliminated from investment and operating costs.
B.   Project Benefits
The project output consists of paper-linerboard and sackkraft--
and roundwood. Turkey has been importing paper for many years, and has re-
cently started importing some roundwood. Since in the absence of the proposed
project these commodities would have to be imported, the economic benefits of
the project are, therefore, the foreign exchange saved by import substitution.
The c.i.f. prices of the project's outputs are given below.
1.   Paper
The major paper exporters have been the USA, Canada, and Northern
European countries. The paper industries in these countries, which until re-
cently had a large unutilized capacity, is now barely meeting demand and
prices have been rising steadily since the second half of 1972. This price
trend is likely to continue: exisitng facilities are operating at practically
full capacity; planned investments fall short of expected growth in demand;
and expenditure on pollution control equipment is raising product cost strain-
ing the industry's financial resources. Furthermore price of major inputs
will continue to rise:  wood, because of felling restrictions and increased
labor cost; fuel and chemicals, because of world shortages.
The steady price increase of linerboard and sackkraft is apparent
from the time series for published Swedish paper export prices which are more
reliable than most. Based on these figures and on the expected world demand



ANNEX 15
Page 2
and supply situation, it is estimated that conservative long-term f.o.b.
Swedish export prices of linerboard and sackkraft, after allowing for the
normal discount, to be about $165 and $210 respectively.

Freight and insurance rates for paper from Sweden to Turkey are
about $26/ton for a fully loaded 12,000 t charter vessel.  Although Turkey
would probably import paper in smaller lots with a concomittant higher freight
rates per ton, we will conservatively use $26/ton as the long-term freight
rated that would be obtained in the absence of the project.
The resulting c.i.f. price for linerboard and multiwall sack paper
are, therefore:
c.i.f. price

Multiwall sack paper    3,304          236

2.   Lumber
The project produces mainly a construction grade of lumber and a
small portion of joinery grade. The border price for lumber produced by the
project is taken equal to the c.i.f. price of Swedish lumber that would give
the same value for the end use. The c.i.f. price of Swedish lumber is obtained
by adding freight and insurance charges to the 1972 FAS Swedish prices given
in the table below. Although 1972 prices are conservative in light of recent
developments, we feel that they reflect long-term trends more accurately than
present prices.
Price History of Rough, Airdried Swedish Sawnwood
3
skr/m  - FAS
Unsorted          Fifths          Unsorted        Fifths
Year           Pine             Pine              Fir            Fir      Sixths
1968           314              241              251             212       161
1969           325              249              257             215       185
1970           334              261              277             244       209
1971           335              276              290             258       219
1972           336              272              280             248       211
Since the project's construction lumber is somewhat inferior to
Swedish Sixth grade sawnwood, the latter's 1972 FAS price -  21 skr/m3 or
$43.20/r3 -  has been deflated by about 10%, reflecting greater re-use of
Swedish lumber. Adding freight and insurance charges, the would-be c.i.f.
price of project construction lumber is therefore estimated at $56.00/m3.
The project's joinery grade is similar to the Swedish unsorted
pine grade valued at skr 336/m3 FAS in 1972. The corresponding c.i.f. price
is about $82.00/i3.
The border prices for lumber are therefore:
c.i.f. price
TL/m3         US $/3
Construction grade  785.0           56.0
Joinery grade      1,148.0          82.0
C.   Project Costs
The investment and operating costs of the project in economic terms
are calculated according to the methodology outline in section A of this Annex.
Calculation details are shown in the attached tables, and except for the two
costs items described below--forestry division operating costs and wood cost-
the tables are self-explanatory.



ANNEX 15
Page 4
1.   Forestry Division Operating Costs
The relevant operating costs in the economic analysis are the incre-
mental operating costs in increasing output from the present 300,000 m3 of
logs/year to about 800,000 m3/year. However, detailed actual operating cost
could not be obtained. Thereforea we have prorated the total operating cost
of the forest component oi the~ project after expansion. This is justified
because the proposed project extends, with minor modifications, existing
labor intensive production methods to a larger area, with little economies
of scale. Prorating, nevertheless, neglects improvement in productivity in
in existing operation induced by project. However, reduction in the economic
rate of return resulting from neglecting this improvement of the project is
negligible.
2.   Wood Costs
Wood used in the industrial part of the project comes from increased
production in the Antalya and Adana-Mersin Areas. Wood qualifies as a domes-
tic good, because high transportation costs makes it uneconomic either to
import or export it: domestic cost of producing and transporting wood to the
border is $18/mi compared to an expected f.o.b. export and import price of
$12-15 m3 and of $30/m3 respectively on a c.i.f. basis.  Furthermore we esti-
mate that an increase in demand by the project will cause an increase in
supply leaving relative prices in the economy unchanged. The economic cost
of the wood is, therefore, equal to the cost of the inputs required to pro-
duce it.
In calculating the cost of wood we have neglected the value of
existing forests. This is justified because not only are Turkey's forests
overstocked with overmature trees, but the project afforestation and refores-
tation components should provide for increased future tree supply.
Since the forestry and industrial components of the project are
combined for purposes of economic analysis, the economic cost of wood supplied
by the project area (Antalya) is already included in the project operating
cost, and we need only to add the economic cost of wood supplied from the
Adana/Mersin area. Because wood production methods are similar in various
fore&* areas in Turkey, the ex-forest cost of wood in the Adaba/Mersin area
were derived from the investment and operating costs in the project area.
Transportation cost for Adana/Mersin to Antalya were then added to arrive
at the wood cost in Antalya.
