SOCIO-ECONOMIC STRUCTURE OF ANTALYA REGION
This Annex briefly describes the economic and demographic situation
in the Antalya area and describes the impact the Project is likely to have
there. It also examines the consequences for the rest of the nation, of
Projects like the Antalya Forest Utilization Project.
Economy of the Region (Basmudurluk)
Agriculture. The most important sector in the economy of the area
is agriculture. Animal husbandry is of great significance for the rural vil-
lagers who rely on goats and sheep for milk, meat, fiber and leather. Com-
mercial agricultural activities are concentrated on the coastal plains and
the Bucak plateau; farming for local use occurs through the region. A 50%
increase in food production from 1 million tons in 1968 to an estimated
1.5 million tons in 1971 has been brought about by sulbstantial increases in
irrigation; successful introduction of glass house horticulture; and an
increase in the intensity of land use. Recorded milk and meat products amount
to 50,000 tons annually but the real contribution of animal husbandry is far
greater as it is consumed locally. Farm animal population in Antalya Province
exceeds 1.75 million and represents a density of 0.83 animals/ha. Some 35,000
tons of cotton and 380 tons of citrus valued at over TL 300 million was exported
overseas from Antalya in unmanufactured form in 1971.
Manufacturing. The major manufacturing activities in the region are
those associated with the processing of agricultural products and the mineral
industry. Wood products are limited to local needs, basically lumber and
wooden boxes. There are several cotton processing and vegetable oil plants,
a chrome ore refinery and mines producing chrome barite, quartzite and bauxite
except for the State box factory and a new 10,000 m3 output sawmill, the wood
processing industry consists of small mills. In 1972 there were over 440
units divided fairly evenly between sawmills, joinery shops and box plants.
The value of manufactured products exported outside Turkey from Antalya in
1971 amounted to nearly TL 60 million.
Tourism. The Antalya basmudurluk with its abundance of archeological
and historical sites, beaches, impressive scenery and mild climate, has sig-
nificant potential for a major tourism industry. Over the past few years,
hotel accommodations, restaurants and other tourist facilities have been
expanded and improved, particularly in the Antalya, Alanya and side areas.
Facilities and attractions include:
Beaches                             Historic Sites
Sand        178 km                  Pre-historic        4
Pebble        8 km                  Greco-Roman        24
Byzantine           4
Beds (1972)   1,700                 Turkish             4



ANNEX 16
Page 2
From 1967 to 1971 the number of tourists were 73,000 annually (26,000 foreign
and 47,000 local). The numbers have remained fairly constant due to limited
transportation facilities from Istanbul and European centers and lack of
accommodations. The Ministry of Tourism has extensive plans for the region
and the NfF has designated three National Park areas, however, development
activity to date is limited.
- Forestry. The forests currently play a supporting role to the major
economical activities in Antalya region and provide a very minor part of the
total income and employment. Production in 1971 (Annex 4) was valued at some
TL 102 million whereas expenditures (mainly wages and salaries) amounted to
TL 74 million. The majority of industrial logs are shipped to other parts
of Turkey for manufacture but the forests do provide the basic supply of fuel
and sufficient industrial wood to meet the construction and box industries'
local requirements. Forest land is used extensively for grazing purposes,
often to the detriment of young trees. With an animal population of 0.83
animals per hectare, it would be extremely difficult to stop this practice,
although it is being controlled in certain limited areas. The role of forests
in the economy of the basmudurluk will be substantially increased by this
project.
Land Use. Practically call level and gently sloping land is either
cultivated or used for urban development. Most of the steep land is brush
covered or forested and unsuitable for agricultural purposes. In spite of
this, significant areas have been cleared by the mountain villagers for
subsistance-level agriculture. This together with goat grazing (over one
million goats recorded in 1968) is a continuing problem for the Forest Service
particularly in remote areas. Erosion is severe on the steep hillside
clearings.
Infrastructure. The central section of the region is reasonably well
developed with good highways, adequate commercial facilities and reasonable
community facilities. The northern, western and eastern areas are less
developed and lack the more basic facilities. The region has a fairly ex-
tensive feeder and access road network, but has a limited main road system.
There are some 500 km of asphalt surfaced roads and 500 km of gravel roads
built and maintained by the State Highway Department. The Ministry of Forests
has built 5,500 km of well constructed and located forest roads.
Sea transport facilities along the coastline are rudimentary in
-.ost locations.  Good port facilities exist at Finike and a new harbor is
being built at Antalya City. Modest docks are in use in Kas and Alanya.
Coastal transport is not well developed and does not provide a practical
alternative to highway hauling within the region.
Frequent, inexpensive bus services are available between all major
towrns and villages along the state highways. As the distance from these
routes increases, bus service is less frequent and is practically non-existent
£or the more remote villages, however the situation will improve as the project
is implemented.



ANNEX 16
Page 3
Regular scheduled air service is provided by the Turkish Airline
between Antalya, Istanbul and Ankara, using modern jet aircraft. There is no
rail service within the basmudurluk.
All major towns have electricity, water, sewage and medical services.
Commercial facilities, banks and other services are well established in the
towns and are extensive in Antalya. Gasoline service stations are frequent
along the major routes but automotive parts stores and repair facilities are
primitive. In the villages and small communities, situated off the main
highways, commercial services are either of a rudimentary nature or non-existent.
In 1971 there were 772 schools in the Province of Antalya catering for over
105,000 students. Practically every community has one or more elementary
shcools.
Population
In 1970, the Antalya Forest Region had a population of some
617,000 or 23 persons/km2. Of these 245,000 (40%) were living in villages
within the forest boundaries and an additional 108,000 (17%) within 10 km of
the forests. Thus, some 57% of the total population live within 10 km of the
forest and have certain statutory and traditional rights to fuelvood and con-
struction wood to graze animals and to work in the forests. Population in
the Antalya Province has increased at 3.6% per annum over the last five years
and the rate in forest villages is even higher (24% over 5 years as compared
with 18% for the basmudurluk as a whole). Data for the period 1940-70 is
shown below.
Percent Change
Census Year    Total Population           Per Annum
1940           256,366
1945           278,178                   1.7
1950           314,442                   2.6
1955           357,568                   2.7
1960           416,130                   3.3
1965           486,910                   3.4
1970           576,828                   3.7

ahown below.

The relative income provided by various economic activities is
summarized below:
Income Sources in Percent
Animal
Village location               Forestry    Agriculture    Husbandry  Other

This shows that agriculture contributes twice as much as forestry to
the forest villagers income while animal husbandry (primarily goats) contrib-
utes over four times as much. Most remote villagers do not live on a cash
economy - basic needs are either home produced or obtained through barter
from other villagers.
The MDF has estimated that over 20,000 people (men and women) would
be available for regular work on forestry operations and an additional
58,000 could work on some types of operations on a seasonal basis.
A member of one of the Banks earlier missions first impressions of
a visit to some forest villages is quoted here as it states very succinctly
the situation as it is today:
"During my visit to Antalya I made an excursion 100 km into the
deep forests stopping at two villages and having informal and
frank discussions with villagers whom we happened to meet.
A senior forestry official who has been working with us on
this project for some years acted as interpreter.
My general impression is that the forest villagers are not
proverty stricken, lead a reasonably comfortable and healthy
life, but are concerned about "mission out" on the benefits
of modern life, such as education for their children and
some spare cash to get consumer goods. The opportunities for
earning cash are very limited and sporadic. The forest
service is the biggest cash employer but has offered general
employment in that particular area for only three years in the
last ten. The ricnest man in one of the villages was in fact
the local forest guard who earns TL 15,000 per year ($1,100
per year). The people would welcome the project if it would



ANNEX 16
Page 5
provide them with more steady employment. They would prefer
to work and live in their present villages although one or two
of the young men felt that they would move to a factory job
in town, but for a premium in wages. There has been a slow
but steady drift of younger people out of the villages partic-
ularly those who have had some education, so that the village
populations have remained fairly constant in the last decade.
Once a villager leaves, he hardly ever comes back."
Likely Impact of the Project
The greatest social impact of the project will be the addition
of nearly 6,000 forest workers and 850 factory workers to the workers presently
employed on a full time basis in the area. This represents a considerable
increase in people having access to a steady cash income, particularly in
the forest region itself, where at present only 7,000 people have steady cash
income. Thus, wage earners in forest activities will increase by 85%, while
wages will increase by 90% to a total of TL 63 million.
Through planning, the increase in forest workers wages will be
evenly distributed among the region thus avoiding the present situation
where forest activities often move from locale to locale bringing a sudden
increase of wealth for a few years followed by reversion to the more normal
non-cash subsistance, agriculture and barter. With the prospect of full
forest utilization it is relatively simple to plan that activities would
take place in all areas of the forests. A slight cost penalty may be in-
troduced by this policy, but it is likely more than offset in the benefits
accruing from greater flexibility in planning production of different
types of logs and different species. Transportation arrangements would
also be more flexible by being evenly spread out.
Money is useful to villagers living in remote areas only to the
extent it allows them to purchase the few essential items they cannot
produce or obtain by barter. This includes agricultural products such as
salt, sugar, tea and tobacco, and manufactured items like shoes, needles,
thread, printed cotton and transistor radios. Cash purchases are made on
occasional trips to market towns - a major trip for many villagers. This
situation accentuates the dependence on farming and animals since commercial
sources of food, leather and woven goods are just not available. Under these
circumstances, a cash income might not be an acceptable alternative to the
present arrangements for a forest family. However, it is likely that the
potential problem will be self-correcting as the more remote villages will
probably not get a sudden increase in forest activities. Rather, they will
come little by little to the remote areas. As forest activities increase
and cash and infrastructure (particularly transport) becomes available, com-
mercial activities are likely to develop. First the traveling salesman will
come with his goods to the villages and later an enterprising villager will
purchase a stock of mixed goods for resale, starting the first retail outlet.



ANNEX 16
Page 6
He will be encouraged by the availability of supplies from the larger centers
that can be brought in by the steady stream of logging, trucks that will return
empty to the forests (and likewise the truckers will be encouraged to seek
return cargo) and by the increasing demand as the villagers start earning
steady wages and move away from subsistance agriculture.
These developments are bound to bring new life to the forest villages.
The prospect of steady employment near the villages may tend to discourage the
drift to the major towns. Village populations might actually grow, putting
new demands on social services like health and education.
Stabilized poPulations and growing skill levels in the villages
could also be a basis on which other enterprising villagers will branch out
into light industrial ventures. As power saws are introduced, the need for
maintenance facilities will spring up and could be handled on a private basis.
The remarkable private enterprise automobile and truck repair business that
exists all over Turkey which keeps a fleet of the most antiquidated and hetero-
genous vehicles in good mechanical condition is a sign that such development
will occur. Increased truck transport will itself create opportunities for
truck repair shops. Cooperatives of villagers may themselves get into the
trucking business hauling logs out of the forested areas and consumer goods
back. It is very likely that the increased logging activities will spur
development of the already widespread system of private logging contractors.
In such ways, secondary economic benefits of the project will
spread.  At present most of the 446 small sawmills in the basmudurluk (of
which 252 are in small remote communities) start with sawlogs as raw material
and produce fairly elaborate goods as door and window frames, and even fur-
nished products. There are, of course, many that produce regular dimension
stock Lumber which others make into elaborate goods - but most of the mills
in remote areas are in fact ioinery shops forced to have a sawmill to provide
the needed lumber. These smaller mills have most of their capital tied up
in sawlog inventory since they can purchase logs only at the infrequent log
auctions. Discussions with the owaers showed that they would clearly like
to be assured of a supply of reasonable priced lumber available when they
wanted it, so that they could direct their energies and capital to making
joinery goods. Just such a supply of lumber will be available from the project
sawmill - so that far from putting the "little guy" out of business, the big
mill, could actually enable him to do better business.
in summary:
-    c-hat local lum7ber dealers and merchants are likely to
arise to handle the distribution of lumber from the
big mill;
-    demand for lumber is likely to increase with increase
in village income;



ANNEX 16
Page 7
-    the empty log trucks can haul back lumber assuring low
transport costs;
-    more sawlogs would be freed for the more efficient
large mill to process.
With secondary socio-economic impacts of this nature taken together
with the primary impacts, as well as the growing demand for agricultural and
tourism labour, one might even postulate a situation of labour scarcity in the
region. In fact in the wood supply contract, the Ministry of Forestry has
already recognized this potential problem by insisting that it may not be
able to debark the logs in the forest (a hand operation) because of future
labour shortages. As a result, SEKA will install mechanical debarking equip-
ment at the plant to be ready for such an eventuality.
There may be a need to consider mechanization of other activities
in the future. For the moment, careful studies have revealed that the present
hand method of logging and replanting cannot be replaced by mechanical systems
on a cost competitive basis. As wages increase and/or labour becomes scarce
or unwilling to tackle the hard manual work, such things as tractor and cable
skidding, tractor drawn terrace preparation and replanting and even hydraulic
felling equipment may have to be considered in addition to the power saws
which are seen as inevitable as a labor-saving device (at equal cost to hand
saws).
Significance for the Rest of Turkey
If this type of forest development spreads to the rest of Turkey
the significance will be immense since it would offset nearly 1/3 of Turkey's
population (12.2 million in 1970).
Here are reasons why the Antalya-type forest development will
spread through Turkey:
-    both SEKA and the Ministry of Forestry advocate the
integrated approach;
-    the pattern has been established by pilot and feasibil-
ity studies;
-    Turkey's demand for forest products, especially paper
is very great. This will demand a more productive and
efficient approach to forestry.
A major social change will therefore occur as the technical methods
of forest exploitation occur. While the techno-economic value of the changes
has been grudgingly granted by conservative elements in the Ministry of
Forestry, they were resisted only because of the realization of the implicit



ANNEX 16
Page 8
social change and the greater freedom for exercising choice that would thereby
come to the forest villagers. Thus there is also a political aspect to the
social impact of the project as freedom from dependence on a part of the
Government' s administration (the forest service) will slowly come to 1/3 of
the electorate. It is no wonder that resistance was encountered among the
more conservative group in the Ministry.
While the widespread impact of these changes seem clear enough, it
is by no means sure how the impact will be felt in different parts of the
country. The situation in Antalya has been described in detail because it
is well-known, but what would it be like in the socalled Anatolia area where
some two million people are classified as forest villagers, but which has few
forests as such and hence only a meager forest production now and is likely
so to have in the future? Surely it will not be the same as in Antalya where
forestry is high in economic importance, but not as high as a basmudurluk, like
the west Black Sea area where forestry practically dominates all economic life.
It might be possible to conjecture that the impact in poor regions
- like mid-Anatolia 1/ - will hardly be felt at all, because increased forest
production is not possible and hence increased local wealth will not occur.
These regions will therefore have to look for other activities then forestry
to ensure their development. The Ministry of Forestry is formulating a devel-
opment program for some 5,000 of the poorest forest villagers over the next
10 years at a cost of some TL 3 million per village. These are contingency
plans to raise the program to 15,000 villages over 20 years if financing can
be found. The mission expressed interest in this program and steps could be
taken to follow this up to see whether the Bank can or should be involved in
the program.
_On the other hand, the impact of new forestry methods in forest-
rich areas like the west Black Sea area 1/ could be quite disjointing to the 2
present way of life. Since this region is also one of the best developed
forest regions - in the sense of road network and other infrastructure -
there are few "remote" villages as in Antalya that will slowly enter the
'3/  in 1972 1inistry of Forestry expenditures _i  Anatolia were TL 108
million and revenue was TL 92 million - a deficit of TL 16 million
2/   In 1972 in the West Bla& Sea area with some 1.1 million forest villagers,
Ministry of Forestry expenditures were TL 327 million, and revenues were
TL 600 million for a surplus of TL 273 million.



ANNEX 16
Page 9
cash economy.  On -t-e contrary those villages are well into the cash economy
through the sale - usually illegal - at market prices of wood that is sold at
marginal cost price to the villagers as their legal right. Higher industrial
wood demands could be met by curtailment of villagers legal entitlements - or
lead at least to increasing the price to market price. This problem could
arise in the execution of the Marmara Region Feasibility Study (see IAnnex 6)
which the Bank is being called on to\finance.-
Despite these potential difficulties which could arise as the social
impact of the Antalya project rises through the forest population, the overall
benefits of such an impact are seen to be positive. The development effect
seems assured even though its exact progress may be uncertain and in need of
continuous monotoring and guidance by the Bank. The Bank's awareness of
possible future problems should not hinder exercising their role.
